Leon:  He's an amazing man.  Of course, he was a faster percussionist in his youth, known through all of Africa as the quickest beat-man in Sudan.  Given his handicap, he's no longer able to play so fast, but his playing has become more meaningful.

Alma:  Kane- your story.  It's important for us to understand what brought you here.

Kane scooped a small handful of berries from the pail.  Heart of the People devoured the cormorant, wrestling the meat into his mouth with his one arm.  In between savage bites, he sharpened his eyeteeth with his hardened fingernails and puffed at his herb-filled pipe.

Kane:  You've been very helpful.  It's only fair that I tell you where I came from.

Leon:  Don't do this for us.  Do it for you.

Kane:  I've always been fascinated with self-destruction.  I was a songwriter- a very famous one.

Leon and Alma shared a look.  Heart of the People ravaged the cormorant, burping and spitting and picking his teeth between squishy chews on the cormorant meat.  The one-armed man's squidgy chewing fueled Kane's nausea.

Kane:  I was one third of a trio called The Scoundrels.  We broke up, and now I'm here.

Alma:  Kane.

Kane:  It wasn't my decision to split.  We had such talent, we really did.  You know, I never meant to involve myself in music.

Leon (picking bits of cormorant from his loopy beard, not really listening to Kane):  Your history is unreliable.  All history is but the crown achievement in literature.

Alma:  Quit it, Leon.  I'm listening to Kane.

Kane:  I wanted to be a poet.  I was both inspired and ashamed with the world.  Just the sight of a woman walking in the street was enough to move me to tears.  If I saw a woman, any woman walking through the street, I wanted to sacrifice my soul to fulfill her every wish.  I fell in love with everyone I ever met.  And I really was a great poet.  I mean I could write.  When I was young, I believed in this concept, which I referred to as the divine beckon, meaning everyone was put here for a reason.

Alma:  Sure.

Leon:  A touch clich, no?

Kane:  I felt I was born to use my words, to create worlds that others could step into and enjoy.  To me, it was like playing God.  But when I looked at the world that I lived in, that very same world that inspired me to turn it into words, I knew no one would ever care to read my poetry.  Shakespeare, Milton, Dante- they wouldn't stand a chance in today's world.  Their talent would remain undiscovered because no one would grant them the attention that their work demands.  The world of a poet is quickly diminishing.  The only modern-day poets who experience any professional fulfillment are those who play music, and I couldn't play a note.  I couldn't sing- my voice sounds funny.

Alma:  I don't think your voice sounds funny.

Leon:  So you learned an instrument?

Kane:  No.  This is my only sorrow.  I was too intimidated to learn an instrument, too scared of success.  Failure, I could handle- people fail.  Success has been the greatest fear I've known.

Alma and Leon shared a look.

Kane:  Then I met Hank.

Heart of the People flashed his red eyes, snapping his jaws at nobody to exercise his teeth.  He belched and continued eating, the foreground to a limitless starry sky.

Kane:  We shared an odd rivalry from the moment I met him, as if we'd known each other all our lives.  We were staged to be showmen- not musical showmen.  I hadn't ever heard him play the piano, but I knew there was something about him.

Alma:  What did you two do before music?

Leon:  He means coitus.

Kane:  No.  Not sex.  Anika was the one who made it about sex.

Leon:  And how did you meet this sexy Anika?

Alma:   Quit it, Leon.

Kane:  There's an era of my life that I can't discuss.  Unfortunately, it's the most exciting era of my life- the era in which I met the man who was to become Hank Adaline and the woman who would control my existence, and we performed our one perfect masterpiece.

Alma:  You're among friends.  You can share.

Kane:  I'd love to tell you.  But I took an oath of silence.  I've never broken that oath.  If Hank were here, he'd tell you.  Hank would tell you anything.  He's no longer the person he was.  That kid was the shyest and most frightened kid I ever met.

Alma:  People change.  From the sound of it, you've changed a great deal.

Kane:  I knew there was something about him, some morbid fear restraining him, but I couldn't pinpoint it.  Then I heard him play the piano.  And that was it.  I was damned.  Hank was already damned.  Anyone who possesses the diseased mind of a genius is eternally damned.  Hank Adaline damned my soul with his mad red piano.  And he was so coy about it, like he didn't even realize that he just altered the course of eternity with ten fingers and eighty-eight keys.  This was the first time he had ever played for anyone other than his parents- you have to understand that we're talking about a genius that was petrified of his own gift.  This is a man who has since been hailed as the incarnate of Beethoven, with a mad touch of Charles Manson, and he had gone eighteen years without ever revealing his talent to anyone.  Perhaps you've heard of us- how long have you been living out here?

Leon:  I left in nineteen ninety-two.

Alma:  Ninety-four for me.

Kane:  Never mind.

Leon:  I assure you that Heart of the People has never heard of you.

Heart of the People sharpened his eyeteeth with long fingernails, his bare feet smelling of dust.

Kane:  Hank was just what I needed for my poetry.  And he needed me too.  I knew it immediately.  He needed me to crack his shell, to outmatch his fears.  Hank was a genius, but he wasn't the intellect he thought he was.  He thought music was powerless.  He thought music was dead, that kids only listen to computers.

Alma:  Computers make music?

Kane:  That's right.

Leon:  Please, I ask you not to speak of current trends.  I don't wish to know the happenings of the world that I absconded.

Kane:  But you play music- you have to be a little interested in the current state of music.

Leon:  I have many interests, and I don't wish to hear of society's use for any of them.  Since you mention my passion for music, I must say that I never respected the music of the West.  You use too many theatrics in your performances.  It becomes something that's no longer about music.

Kane:  You wouldn't like The Scoundrels.  You especially wouldn't like me.  I was the master of theatrical performance, The Jester- I made Bowie look like a coma patient.

Leon:  What's a Bowie?

Alma:  Don't mind Leon.  Please continue, Kane.

Kane:  Hank Adaline hated the idea of computerized music.  He thought the world had devolved, that it was a terrible time for music, a terrible time for artists, for Rebels.  I told him that time is what you make of it.  He had an affinity for breaking conventions in an original way, which was quickly becoming a difficult accomplishment in the industry.  But Hank's mad red piano revitalized Rebellion.

Leon:  Stop this.  I ask you not to speak of trends.

Kane couldn't stop.  He couldn't help himself.  When Kane spoke about The Scoundrels he spewed all thoughts in verbal vomit, because these were the first ears he'd found since he tattooed himself and disappeared, and because life had always been a conspiracy against Kane- Kane Hailand meets his boyhood hero and all he could talk about are his own hang-ups.

Kane:  It was more of a rebirth than revitalization.  Hank's brand of music is unlike any that came before it- this is the important thing to know.  And there was a time when I was the only one who saw this in him.  So I told him what I believed to be lies to convince him to use his gift.  He needed someone to translate his genius, and I needed a sound to portray my words.  Together, we would challenge the world.  But it didn't happen that way.  It couldn't.  The Scoundrels' legacy is that we were the greatest tri-fold musical partnership the world had ever known, but what people fail to notice is that we never saw ourselves as partners.  We were the greatest of enemies, brilliant scoundrels who were stuffed into a trio together because we were the only ones who could compete with one another.  We were competitive in everything we did.  That's what made us so great.  Scoundrels was the only name that ever fit us all.

Alma: So you wrote the lyrics, Hank played the piano, and this Anika- what did she do?

Kane:  Just as today's poet will have difficulty reaching an audience without musicianship, so will a musician who can't sing.  Hank and I began composing together.  I had written the verse to Train of Power, my first piece, my premature peak, my magnum opus- a 252 line poem (2+5+2), 9 syllables per line, voiced by alternating narrators who jab in couplets.  Hank composed an accompaniment during a productive four-day stretch.  He encapsulated my words in sound, that mad genius.  It was beyond any sort of reality.  I knew that we were on the verge of something that would snap history in two.  But it wasn't complete, it needed something more.  Hank told me he knew a singer.  He said it would be a problem to get her to work with us, but she had an unrealized potential that he was coaching without her knowledge.  Hank Adaline was always this way- it's difficult to understand the enormity of his influence on you.  He said her voice could lure a man's soul from whatever side of despair to the other side and back, a siren nymphet in the church choir.  He introduced her to me as his girlfriend.  She was wacky, she was eccentric, and he treated her awfully- and I was instantly in love.  She thought in 9's, everything in 9's.  If she sneezed once, eight more always followed.  She walked in 9's, brushed her teeth in 9's, breathed her violet smoke in 9's- except when she was singing.  When we were on stage, she was free.  I can't imagine the mania she must've endured offstage.  Everything she saw was violet.  The whole world was one purple dungeon.  My love was demonized, she was a monster- anybody who tries to squander a gift like that voice is a monster.  But I loved her.

Ash crackled in the fire as smoke rose into night.

Alma:  What do you mean by squandering her gift?

Kane:  People traveled the world to hear her voice.  They offered her millions of dollars to hear their favorite songs sung from her magical throat.  I told you that my purpose in writing poetry was to turn the world into words.  Anika changed that.  After meeting Anika, the only reason I ever composed a word was to hear her sing.  She never wanted any of it.  I've never seen her without a burning violet cigarette between in her lips.  She has cancer.  She's dying.  And still she smokes.

Alma:  Poor thing.

Kane:  She wanted it, begged for it.  She said she wanted to die in slow pain.

Alma:  My, that's awful.  Even with the prospect of death, you three couldn't put your differences aside.

Leon:  Three geniuses will never fit their egos in one room.

Kane:  We weren't three geniuses.  Hank was the only genius.  Anika wasn't a genius in any sense.  She was just the greatest talent anyone had ever seen.  There were serious studies conducted in major universities testing whether her voice had healing qualities.  Hank Adaline is maddened in craft- he hears things that aren't entirely of this world and he translates them through a red piano.  They have a word for people like this- I seem to have lost it.

Leon:  Clairaudient.

Kane began a story that he had often rehearsed in his head, a monologue he once wrote in case he ever encountered an alien species, a fantasy that Kane will readily admit to be both ridiculous and paranoid, but it became useful among his present company.

Kane:  The tragedy of Hank's genius is that he's always in limbo between two worlds- the world of sound and our material world.  It's difficult for the genius to know which world is real, and how to escape the one that isn't real.  The life of a genius is a pitiful existence.  Not that I can attest.  I was never a genius- but I was a superior intellect as a young man.  I fooled Hank into working with me by outsmarting him.  No, I have to call it what it was- it was deception.  I methodically devised an intellectual scheme just above Hank's intelligence level, and I didn't unveil my secret until we performed our first composition, our masterpiece- Train of Power.  I wish I could go into more detail, but this is the era of my life that is sworn secret- the formation of The Scoundrels, the performance of Train of Power, the Violet 9.  I beat him in a game he didn't realize we were playing until he lost.  Can you imagine the feeling- the bitterness of loss without the thrill of competition?  Pure loss.

Leon:  Someone could be pulling the same trick on you as we speak, and you'd have no idea.

Kane:  I don't love anything enough to have a trick pulled on me.

Leon:  We'll see.

Kane breathed for effect, drinking in the stars.

Kane:  I deceived Hank out of both fear and love.  I feared that he would leave me if I didn't do something irreversible, and I feared that his talent would go unseen without my love and guidance.  The reasons I chose to reveal my victory over him at the close of our one perfect performance were purely selfish.  Hank and I led a society called the Violet 9- this is the society I have alleged my silence.  We did away with our society in that moment in which Good and Evil intersect on their perpendicular paths to the ultimate, the one true balance of all mankind manifested absolutely in the pure violet of a slender female singer- Train of Power.  The one piece I would choose to leave as my legacy is the one piece of mine the world has never seen.

Alma:  Train of Power.  Could you recite it for us?

Kane:  It's gone.  The train has passed.  The verse I wrote only applied to the moment in which it was performed, and will never again hold true.  That's why it was perfect.  We performed it just once- never even recorded it.

Alma:  Surely you must have practiced before the performance?

Kane:  Hank Adaline and Anika Handel don't practice.

Leon:  You put your greatest work above public scrutiny.  Bravo, Kane.

Kane:  It wasn't a conscious choice to forgo recording.  The piece really only worked as a live performance.  The finale was bold.  It was beyond criminal, not to be repeated.  I have blood on my hands.

Leon:  Was it worth it?

Kane:  No.  But if I could go back, I'd do it again.

Heart of the People burped his satisfaction.  Kane watched the motion of the man's stumpy arm, curiously eyeing the holstered blade at his hip.

Alma:  So what came after Train of Power?

Kane:  How do you follow perfection?  We were forever changed, new beings trying to cope with the madness.  We wrote three albums to coincide with our individual personas.  I invented the character of Kane Hailand in I am the Jester, O yea, O yea, the story of a godlike Jester who wears a hand-painted eye patch.  Kane had complete control of light as well as the amazing ability to summon creatures of the wild.  Red Chaos was a particularly beautiful portrait of Hank- the red devil running barefoot and cursing, sparking fire with his palms, wearing red in the dark just to fool you- another tale told by two conflicting narrators who were the closest of friends and the greatest of enemies.  And then Anika's Anthem-Vile Violet Inviolate Violence, or V.V.I.V., the tragic saga of three unrequited loves.  We became stars, international icons.  Our live performances enlivened us with the ability to be the myth.  There was one thing The Scoundrels ever agreed on- the superiority of a live show to a studio record.  The studio was where we gathered our thoughts, but the stage was where we explored.  Art should not be silenced and stored on file to be played, paused, rewound- controlled.  Art should be like a stench, inescapable in the air.

Leon:  Precisely.  It's not about preservation- it's about exploration.

Kane:  For a while, you would've thought we controlled the world.  And we would have, if we could ever control ourselves.

Leon grunted his disagreement.

Alma:  Unlike Leon, I love a great performance- you know it was the Greeks who invented theatre.  Athenian actors would perform with gigantic two-sided masks.  To express a change in emotion or well-being, the actor would flip his mask.  The Greeks didn't allow women to act.  They thought that women lack the capacity to convey emotion properly.

Kane:  The Greeks never met Anika.

Alma:  Tell me, Kane, if you loved her so much, why didn't you do anything about it?

Kane:  She knew how I felt.  But she was so controlled by Hank.  He treated her like trash and she knew it.  She stuck with him.

Alma:  Why wouldn't she go with you if she recognized your love?

Kane:  It's hard to explain.  I don't know if I've stressed this enough- I feel like everything I've ever done is a part of some greater competition with Hank Adaline.  Everything.  Even this, right now, talking to you, when he's on the other side of the globe and we'll probably never see each other again- I'm still competing with him in my head.  The only reason he hung onto Anika was because he knew I loved her.  This was his way of beating me, and I'll never forgive him for it.

Alma:  I see why Hank didn't leave Anika, but why wouldn't she leave him if he treated her so horribly?

Kane:  Because he ruined her.  She was once perfect and pure, a virgin contralto in her church's choir.  Then he destroyed her.  And she continues to destroy herself.  I've had a rough time lately.  There are things I've forgotten.  I'm somehow mentally incapacitated.  I recognize this.  There's one conversation I can never forget.  After a particularly emotional concert in Paris, she approached me backstage.  She said she knew that I could save her.  She said that's what she was afraid of- being saved.  Because then what?  I save her and then what happens?  She said she'd never be able to return the favor.  I told her that the favor had already been returned, that I was the one who owed her, that I was meant to save her- the divine beckon.  She just puffed her violet smoke, kissed me the loneliest kiss, and turned away.

Leon:  What you're not seeing, Kane, is that you were thinking only of your own pain.  You never thought of her, of her pain.

Alma:  So you broke up the band?

Kane:  No.  Hank broke up the band.  He ditched his piano for a mad red turntable.  He spent his whole career disparaging electronic music, and then he adopted it.  The fans aren't approving.  He has a new fan base now- a younger crowd.  They call him the hottest DJ to ever spin a record.  I don't think they really understand his work.  From a lyricist's perspective, he's too consciously clever.  He curses too much.  There's no message in his electronic music- there's such a limited range of feeling.  He needs me to filter his genius, and the world needs him back on the piano.

Alma:  Doesn't Hank notice the reaction against his new music?

Kane:  I guess not.  I really can't say.  Maybe he didn't want to split the money anymore- he was a thief.

Alma:  Kane, you're too young to be an old man.  To me, you look more like a sweet boy.

Heart of the People spoke.

Massaging his throat, Leon noisily gathered his spit and hocked a fat wad into an empty wooden cup.  He passed the cup to Alma, who followed suit.  Alma passed the wooden cup to Kane.

Alma:  Spit.

Kane obliged, unsure of what he was doing or why he was doing it.  He passed the cup to Leon, who passed it to Heart of the People.  With his one hand, Heart of the People upturned the wooden cup and swished the saliva cocktail around his mouth as if rinsing with mouthwash.  Leon explained to Kane that the extra saliva cleans the mouth and deters tooth decay.
When he was finished, Heart of the People spewed the amalgam of spit into the sand.  Using his one hand as support, he stood from his stone throne.  He pointed his one index finger into the sky and tracked an invisible satellite to his right, mentally calculating the angles.  Lowering his finger cautiously, so as to keep his invisible angle constant, he muttered something to Leon, who quickly snagged three branches from the fire and placed them in arrow formation in line with Heart of the People's finger.  Heart of the People lifted his drum and disappeared into his tent.

Leon:  He said we set forth at dawn.  There is another river about twenty kilometers northeast.

Kane:  Why not stay here?

Leon:  He spotted an incoming storm from the southeast earlier today.

Kane:  How does he know that?

Leon:  As an American child would study history or mathematics, the children in his tribe studied scavenging and astrology.  Be thankful that you're healthy for our move tomorrow.  If you were still unconscious, there's a chance we may not have been able to bring you with us.  Not knowing about your musical background, we almost certainly would've left you.

Kane:  I stood up at his wedding.

Leon:  What?

Alma:  He's talking about Hank and Anika.

Leon:  Oh.  What did you do once you stood up?

They shared a laugh, the first since Kane began his story.

Alma:  Do you think you can be happy here, Kane?

Kane:  I don't know.

Leon:  Do you know what happiness is?

Kane:  I think so.  I imagine happiness to be like love-

Leon burst into laughter.

Alma:  You have a fascinating story, Kane.

Kane:  The story of how I became lost in violent wilderness.

Alma studied Kane with a puzzled expression.

Alma:  Wilderness isn't violent.  It's a blank canvas.

Kane:  For some of us.

Alma:  What I'm saying to you, Kane, is that you could've seen that wilderness as anything.  The land is whatever you want it to be.  You could've said it was lovely, lonely, happy, sorrowful or amazing- but you saw it as violent, and that comes from a part of you that is very scared.

Leon:  There's one more thing Heart of the People wishes I tell you.

Kane:  What is it?

Leon:  Your Anima.

Kane:  My Anima?

Leon:  Yes.  But first I'd like to tell you more of my story.  I understand you're familiar with my past, but I want to clarify the story from my perspective, so that you don't think of me as a copout.

Kane:  No, I-

Alma reached forth, placing two warm fingertips over Kane's lips.

Alma:  It's important that we know each other's stories, Kane.

Kane relaxed, and listened.

Leon:  My suicide, in many ways, was not faked.  When I chose to stage my own death, it was just that- my death.  I would never again live as Baltasar Kanaan.  You're familiar with my familial ties to the Bonabali aristocracy?

Kane:  Yes.

Leon:  My father was a member of the cabinet under the republic.  He was a strict parent, yet caring in odd ways.  I never experienced the paternal love that I read about in books, and yet I know he did love me.  He expressed this by grooming me to become the next leader of our country.  Fathers have visions for their sons- you understand.

Kane:  I'm an orphan.  I never met my parents.

Leon:  You've had another pair of man and wife to deliver you anguish.  My first distinct memory was of walking through the streets of Polabhid to my tutor's home.  This was the first time I was permitted to walk without a guardian of any sort- I believe there was some emergency that occupied my father's staff, but I was too young to understand at the time.  As with any child, I was accustomed to going places without questioning where I was being taken.  I never wondered where I was headed, never noticed the path I was taking to get where I was going.  Walking through this wonderful city, alone for the very first time, I noticed things that I had always ignored under the guiding grip of my guardian's hand.  There were so many people, and all of them seemed to know me.  They would wave their hands and call my name, offering me food.  Could it have been that they had been waving all along- I'll never know, but on that day I felt loved.  For the first time, my feet were led by my desire.  I walked past the fruit carts, weaving through the maze of the bazaar, waving, smelling the greens and the spices in their flour sacks, dazzled by the colorful fabrics and jewelry, the kettles, the aroma of hookah in every variety, the fruits and baking bread, the fish, ah yes, the coiled pastries, the dishware, and all the people breathing in the tunes of conversation.  I had never noticed any of this before, and it was all so marvelous.  At the time, I was about as tall as a grown man's waist.  I recall thinking it strange how the people walked- not urgently, as my father's men, but with a steady ease.  A generous fellow offered me a spinach fatayer.  He called me by name.  I can still see him in my mind.  He wore a thobe and a grey moustache.  His friend wore the strangest beard I had ever seen, much like the one I wear today.  I accepted the bread and thanked him kindly.  As I turned to continue my strange journey, my body stopped moving.  My heart jumped.  I forgot to breathe.  I nearly choked, dropping the half-eaten fatayer.  But my ears were too occupied to hear the mangy plop of pastry in the dirt.  I heard the voice of God.  The man with the strange beard tapped my shoulder and offered me a stick.  Blow into it, he said.  I examined the instrument- a hallowed reed with seven holes.  He held the ney to my mouth and placed his fingers methodically over select holes.  To this day, I am amazed to think how strong he must have been- to hold those notes for someone else to play.  He tipped the ney closer to my body so that it was almost vertical against my chest.  I blew, but heard nothing.  The air that I blew was just wind.  Relax your tongue, he said.  Your tongue is a snake.  Charm it.  I put the ney back into my lip and blew.  Nothing.  Blow like you would a candle, he said.  It must have taken me hundreds of tries to produce a sound.  But when I did, it was magical.  It was the first time I had taken something from inside my body and turned it into something beautiful to live in the outside world.  The bearded man with the magical instrument introduced himself as Leon.  I had never heard this name before.  Leon.  It sounded so strange and fun to a child's ears.

Alma:  Leon is Greek for Lion.

Leon:  He gave me his ney as a gift, and he told me to come back for lessons, free of charge.  I walked home, giddy as a puppy, dancing with my ney, blowing uselessly into its holes.  When I approached my father's mansion, I registered the gravity of what I had done.  I ditched my tutor, avoided my studies- for music.  Music, my father always said, was for the servant to play and for the gentleman to enjoy.  Music served no purpose in politics.

Kane:  He's never heard of The Scoundrels- high society loved Hank enough to overlook his open mockery of everything they did.

Leon:  With a name like The Scoundrels I don't imagine my father would approve.  Understand also, Kane, that I was raised under a Muslim regime.  Everyone I respected preached the scripture to me, and no matter how I tried to believe, I just couldn't.  Try to imagine the effect this can have on a child- I was always told that my independent beliefs were wrong.

Kane leaned in, paying rapt attention.

Leon:  I hid Leon's ney in our backyard almond orchard.  Beet-red, trying to hide my guilt, I entered the house.  I recall my father waiting at the door with a leather strap in hand.  He had spoken with the tutor, who messaged when I didn't show for my lesson.  I never saw Leon again.  But I finally had motivation to complete my studies.  I developed a routine.  I'd pack a bag with books, paper, pens, and various supplies, and embark for the almond orchard.  I was disciplined.  I did not touch the ney until my studies were complete.  This is how I trained myself to think quick enough to complete work on challenging theorems in a fraction of the time it took others to complete with less quality.  The more success I saw, the more freedom I was given.  I stomached all of it- the studies, the religion- because I had my ney, and that's what mattered.

Alma moved toward Kane, leaning her head on his shoulder.  He felt her silk hair stream down his chest, the wildflower in her ear, blossoming in marvelous scent.

Alma:  This story becomes more wonderful every time you tell it, Leon.

Leon:  As I was an only son, I was exempt from military service.  I grew older, and my accomplishments, as well as my recognition for these accomplishments, became greater.  Aiming to appease my father, I began to publish papers.  These publications were held in great esteem by my country.  My father was proud.  When I graduated as valedictorian of the national university, he said one day you will lead Bonabal to glory.  He then placed his hand on my head.  It was the first time he ever touched me.

Kane:  Where was your mother?  They never really write much about her.

Leon:  She saw enormous potential in me and she wanted me to do whatever I wished.  She never focused on my accomplishments, but my happiness.  What could she do?  My father's word was law.  With the publication of my dissertation, Vagabonds Allow Glimpses Into Noumenal Apparitions, or, as I call it, The Orifice, I gained an international reputation.  The western world was impressed to read a distinguished work on Shakespeare from an Arab student.

Kane:  I remember.  I was a child at the time.  You formed my whole worldview.

Leon:  My next move was to design a theoretical device that would effectively record an individual's thoughts.  The technology was not yet available, but the idea was that the atomic recorder could be injected into the nervous system and travel to the brain, guided by a computer to pick up neural signals in the frontal lobe.  Upon publication of my design, high-ranking military officials approached me to design a camera disguised to imitate the exact appearance, texture, and motor capabilities of a housefly.  I told them it was impossible to imitate the motion of the wings, which was a lie- I thought the technology was too dangerous.  My father was disappointed.  He said I let the nation down.

Kane:  I've heard fathers are often disappointed.  That's why I never understood the desire for kids.

Alma shuddered.  Kane felt the vibrations of her shaking body, her head still resting comfortably on his shoulder.

Leon:  As I gained success in the eyes of the nation, my father began to share more of his government knowledge with me- to involve me in decisions, to teach me how to operate.  The more he told me, the more revolted I became.  My father wanted to go to war- he was planning a campaign that proposed the invasion of the Talyun.  The Talyun was a much-disputed region on the border between Bonabal and Natspatsu.  The territory lies on an elevated terrain, granting a keen military advantage to whoever controls the region.  The Talyun was then controlled by Natspatsuvian troops.  I don't know who runs the Talyun today, I don't know if the Talyun still exists- I don't much care, as I have extricated myself from anything pertaining to the conflict.

Kane:  Why did he want to go to war?

Leon:  At the time, there was much talk in Bonabal about privatization.  Our country had nationalized most enterprises, and the influx of money was fortuitous for those involved in government.  If the government were to permit privatization, my father predicted that he would lose large portions of his income to free enterprise.

Kane:  How would a war with Natspatsu solve a problem that existed within his own country?

Leon:  The invasion of the Talyun was not the beginning of his plans- it was the culmination.  First, he said, Bonabal must become autarkic- meaning the nation would become completely self-sufficient, abolishing trade with foreign nations.  With autarky comes national pride.  Independence breeds confidence, my father said.  When designed correctly, he believed this proposal would be unanimously supported in parliament- whether or not his predictions were fulfilled, I never discovered.  Under his plans, the citizens would be forewarned of the plan for autarky.  They would be told that they were to temporarily go without certain luxuries as a sacrifice for their country- understand that my father used the Arabic equivalent of the word luxury to describe necessities like food.  Ultimately, my father believed, the citizens would support his cause, because to be truly autarkic, it was necessary for Bonabal to withdraw its troops from Sevonim for the first time in over fifteen years, a laudable political move.  My father planned to market his scheme as a doctrine for peace.

Absorbing Leon's story, the baffled Kane Hailand forgot to breathe.

Leon:  Bonabal, at least at that time, did not possess the internal resources to function self-sufficiently.  Autarky would fail- only government officials would be privy to this part of the plan.

Kane:  So where does war come in?

Leon:  In our failed economy, my father would support the proposition that Bonabal invade the Talyun to seek the advantage of Natspatuvian resources.  The Bonabali government would inoculate the citizens to believe that the invasion was an order from God to recapture land that was rightfully theirs, according to their own right to subsist.  War would ensue.

Kane:  How would this help your father?

Leon:  The role of government only strengthens under wartime economy.  A rationing system would be implemented in order to invest more capital in the military.  With this increased control over the people, talks of privatization would be muddled under more pertinent issues.  A war, even one in which his country lost, would make my father richer.  A victory would make him a hero.  The way he saw it, he couldn't lose.

Kane:  What did you say to this?

Leon:  I voiced the opinion that I was against it.  From a practical standpoint, it was a poor international move.  Engaging in war with Natspatsu would've cost us relations with the Americans, which we held dearly.  More importantly, I didn't want men to die, on either side of the border, so that my father could get paid.  My father said this is how it is.  He said you have to look out for yourself or you'll end up in the slums.  I said that although Bonabal wasn't powerful enough to save the world, we were strong enough to save ourselves and inspire others to do the same.  He said we need war, that war is a way out, a path to freedom.  I wanted no part of this.  I conducted my clone studies in secret while pretending to pursue theories on Shakespeare, playing my ney all the while.  And then the world saw my gory suicide.  If you can't trust the man who begot you, whom can you trust?

Kane:  That's an incredible story.

Leon:  Perhaps today my father Usman Kanaan is a king.  Perhaps he is dead and forgotten.  I don't wish to know.

Kane faltered, unnerved to hear that Leon really didn't know his father's fate.  Though he tried to focus on Leon's story, Kane couldn't help but wonder if he should tell Leon what happened to his father.

Leon:  You have to understand that where I am from, family is holy.  To do what I did- even if I went back, they'd consider me a dead man.  In fact, if they knew I was alive, there would be a price on my head.  I needed a disguise.  I figured who better to be than Leon, the man who gave me the wonderful gift of the ney?

Kane (hesitant):  So how'd you get here?

Leon:  How I came to be a part of Heart of the People's family is a more important story.  By sharing this story, I'm giving you an opportunity of the utmost importance.

Kane:  Okay.

Leon:  Opportunity is only an opportunity if you recognize it as opportunity- it is a power that can be abused.

Kane:  What opportunity is this?

Leon:  Your Anima.


* * *



Track 3: The Tribe Dances


Alma threw several more branches onto the fire.  Reaching behind Heart of the People's vacant throne, she lifted the cooling pail of purified water and handed it to Kane.

Alma:  Be careful- it's hot, but it's safe.

Kane thanked Alma.  He sipped the steaming water, burning his lips and warming his chest.  His shadow danced in the fire.

Leon:  Tell me, Kane, in their stories of my suicide, did they mention that I had a ney in hand?

Alma:  Not so much water Kane, we're running low.

Kane:  I never heard of a ney until today.

Leon:  That was my father's doing.  He bore the shame of my suicide, but he couldn't stomach the shame of my passion.

Kane gulped the water, trying to think of anything to sidestep the fate of Usman Kanaan.

Kane:  You knew that already.

Leon:  Yes.  I did.

Kane:  Where did you go when you left?

Leon:  All facets of my escape were meticulously prefigured.  After killing my clone, I disguised myself to match the identity I fabricated.  Though I aspired to be Leon, it would be years before my beard could grow long enough to be knotted in such fantastic fashion- the disguise I used looked nothing like the man you see today.

Kane:  So where'd you go?

Leon:  In my readings, I encountered several articles about a Sudanese rebel who was breeding a new religion through music.  It was said that he and his followers freed a throng of child slaves as they were trafficked between work camps.  Legend said that this man nurtured and exalted the children, providing for their needs.  He taught that music is the sole deity, that everything is music.  Music, my ney, had always been my only love- I was mortally ashamed of this.  My father's attitude had convinced me that there was something wrong with my passion, that I was sick.  To hear of a man who shared my love, who worshipped music as I always had, was to learn that heaven existed.  Understand also that Sudan had recently fell victim to a military government- by inoculating the people with fresh ideas, this man was risking his life.  This man became the only being on the globe worthy of my travels.  He was known through all of Africa as the fastest beat-man in Sudan.  People spoke of his red flashing eyes.  He flashed his eyes to conceal his genius- no one could ever hope to understand him through his eyes.  He had no name.  He didn't believe in names- only sound.

A fall of Alma's brunette ringlets rolled gently down Kane's chest as she yawned, nestling into his shoulder.

Leon:  I met a driver at the airport in Khartoum who claimed to possess a canvassed camp Land Cruiser and an intimate knowledge of the man I wished to see.  He required a trivial fee in exchange for knowledgeable inquiries regarding the famed percussionist.  This same driver would later hold a bayonet to my throat while robbing me of all belongings, leaving me stranded in the wild landscape.

Kane:  Your driver robbed you?

Leon:  He left me with nothing.  Most descendants of wealth are ill-equipped to survive in nature, as they thrive only in a self-patterned environment- an environment in which the so-called wealthy have no control will expose their weakness for others to crush their souls.

Leon paused.  Alma burrowed into Kane, who was taken with the prospect of a woman being so amorous- Anika never laid her head on his shoulder.  Leon continued, leaning forward.

Leon:  We are all one, Kane.  We all have similar potential.  It is unholy money that muddles this notion.  I was interested in seeking a life in which money was completely absent.  In an environment free of money, I believed I could truly understand human nature.

Kane:  How has that worked out for you?

Leon:  I'm learning every day.

Leon snuck a momentary glance toward Heart of the People's cloth tent.

Leon:  In preparation for my escape, I became a student of survival skills.  I realized that the circumstances of my flight created a framework in which any situation may possess me- it was imperative that I be primed for this inevitable anything. I didn't panic when my driver robbed me.  Instead, I scoured the land to craft myself a ney.  Only with my ney was I complete- without it I was incapable.

Kane:  You made the ney from parts of nature?

Leon:  It was a simple, yet tedious process.  The ney is really a precursor to your western flute.  To begin, I found cane- this step proved to be easy, as Sudan had the third largest sugar industry in Africa at the time.  With nothing on my person but the small knife that my thieving driver overlooked in his pat-down, I exhaustedly cut the cane at the appropriate length.  Some say I should've knifed the driver, as I am a lethal throw with a knife, but the truth is that I embraced the spontaneity.

Kane:  You're a knife-thrower?

Leon:  I was until I met Heart of the People.

Kane:  Why only until you met Heart of the People?

Leon:  Because he taught me that violence is the weapon of the weak.  We don't speak with knives- we speak with music.

Kane:  Wait- they let you on a plane with a knife?

Leon:  Why should the airline know I had a knife?

Kane:  The world has since become consumed by paranoia.

Leon:  While completing the ney, I spent several lunar cycles in the tent of a generous goatherd known only as Tree.  I passed my time crafting seven holes in precise size, shape, and relative arrangement.  This proved especially difficult with only a small knife.  When alas I left Tree's home with my ney, my beard and hair were significantly longer.  Looking into the waters of the Nile, the reflected face could never be mistaken for Baltasar Kanaan.

Kane:  I'll say.

Leon:  It's rare that a wanderer finds plant-life in the desert, but I needed a dependable wood to carve the end-piece for my ney.  My kind host Tree directed me toward a hashab.  He warned me not to eat its fruit- though it may be delicious, it may also be toxic.  This is an integral instruction, Kane- don't eat any fruits you're unfamiliar with.  Promise me.

Kane:  I promise.

Leon:  When I found the hashab, I crafted my endpiece.  Memory as my only guide, I relied greatly on my hands to sense when the carving was complete, massaging every particle of the reed until it felt as perfect as Leon's ney.

Kane:  That's incredible.

Leon:  As you know, all artists must eventually confront their dreaded end- she became perfect, my ney was complete.  I blew into the mouthpiece.  The note sang forever- I can still hear it today as the song of the land, the fruits of my love, a sweet soul-bred whistle calling for the man with the red flashing eyes.  This was the first time I touched freedom.

Alma yawned again.  Resting her head on Kane's shoulder, he could feel her yawn as if it was his own.  This strange feeling prompted him to share her yawn, inhaling her sweet flowery scent, comfortable out of contentedness rather than necessity.

Leon:  I traversed the sands.  The desert is the only land that allows a man to think clearly.  I took this lucidity for delusion, but delusion can expose phenomena unnoticed in sobriety- delusion allows access to parts of the soul that I otherwise would have never known existed.  When a man is alone with his self, he learns just how little he knows about his own lonely being.

Kane:  What did you eat while you were out there?

Leon:  There were many nomads, people who wandered the land through their ancestry, and they always offered me a home.  They didn't have much, but they were good people, providing me with food, water, shelter, and always a clever hint about the man I sought.  And, as already mentioned, I was deadly with my knife.

Alma lifted her head from Kane's shoulder, rubbing a hand over her stomach.  Leon faltered, alarmed.  She assured him that she felt all right.  The fire flickered dimly.

Leon:  Finally I found him.  I didn't know how much time had elapsed since my escape.  My beard, which had grown several inches, was my only measure of time- I had long since quit my tally of the sunrise, as time is useless in the desert.  Though I had never visited Heart of the People's village, I recognized it immediately from the joyful rhapsodies that sang from the slopes.  His tribe lived in grass huts, built inside the earth.  The huts provided an efficient means of living in a land with no electricity- living in the earth provides natural light and moderate temperatures.  Our days were cooled and our nights were warmed by mother soil.  The huts also served to shield us from dust and insects in the desert.  It was so beautiful, Kane.  To walk the row of huts was to peruse a great museum of sound- every hut I passed emitted a new inspired treasure, unique yet equally as luring as the preceding gem of sound, urging me forth into this musical arcadia, thousands of unfamiliar instruments and buzzing insects all tuned to the beautiful channel of childlike chatter.  I could detect several dozen distinct languages within the village, many of which I could not comprehend.  Heart of the People encouraged all to join his tribe, no matter their culture, no matter their language- music, he declaimed, shall unite us all.

Kane:  This is the guy who spit on my forehead?

Leon:  Certainly.  When I arrived in the village, a toothless child led me to my leader.  None of the tribesmen wore clothing.  I recall streamlines of smoky ambrosia leaking from the musical huts.  This delicious smoke, I later learned, was a perfume that the women bathed in each morning.   The toothless boy led me through the hall of musical smoke to see the face that I had only seen in dreams.  Heart of the People sat calm in his great hut, beating his drum to the rhythm of life.  He balked when I approached, divining into my soul as if he had been awaiting my presence.  Though we had never met, I believed that he knew everything about me.

Leon paused.  Kane waited for Leon to continue, but Leon seemed to have forgotten his role as raconteur, eyes closed and dreaming in the night.

Alma:  Do you hear that?

Kane:  What?

Leon:  Listen closely to the silence.

A wave of nothingness resonated through Kane's eardrum- so quiet, so beautiful, mesmerizing, overpowering, the great waves of silence grew to open the chambers of Kane Hailand's mind as they hadn't been opened in ages.  He leaned his head back and smiled.

Leon:  Music, sound, is about silence- that's the first thing he ever told me.

Kane deconstructed the aphorism in his head.

Leon:  With so much hatred in the world, it's easy for us to become confused- but there's a feeling that you can only get from music.  It's a feeling that everyone and everything has fallen into place as the universe has meant it.  That feeling is the dance of passionate reality- everything else is just discord.  Passion, says Heart of the People, is truth.  Those who do not attain passion may be too frustrated to attain truth.

Kane:  I don't follow.

Leon:  You will- when you find your Anima.

Kane:  I still don't understand what an Anima is.

Alma:  Be patient, Kane.

Leon:  After encountering many unfamiliar languages, I was pleased to find that Heart of the People spoke Arabic- although his dialect was one I had never encountered.

Kane:  I know the feeling.

Leon:  This may be difficult for you to imagine, but he was a great orator.  Those fortunate enough to hear him speak were forever changed by the experience.  What he said and how he said it didn't matter so much- he was always able to reach a certain hope inside of you that often goes untouched.  He uttered only truth on the most fundamental level, always ahead of his audience to blaze their path with spoken wisdom.  Many people migrated from distant villages in hopes that he could heal their sick children with mystical love.

Alma:  Tell Kane about the code.

Leon:  He established a code for the tribe.  Of course, much of this code has been abandoned since our days in Sudan, but the instructions I am about to list are rules that we still follow.  Most importantly, never travel by night.  Ever.  Only the beggars, the prideless, come out at night.  We do not beg.  Beggars cling to the false hope that they will one day succeed in the governed society.  We have removed ourselves completely from this society, and therefore we will not beg them for alms that we don't seek. You understand?

Kane nodded.

Leon:  Do not eat fruit you are not familiar with.  This is obvious.

Kane nodded.

Leon:  We spend our days scavenging for supper.  When we travel as a trio, two of us scavenge while the third watches the camp.  You will figure into the rotation as Heart of the People wishes.

Kane nodded.

Leon:  We eat one meal a day.

Kane:  Why just one?

Leon:  Because one is plenty.  Why do things the way they've always been done if they've never been done correctly?

Kane nodded.

Leon:  Even then, Heart of the People was adamant that we do not hunt with weapons.  We scavenge- we don't use weapons.  I've abandoned throwing knives long ago.  The reason for this part of the code is in the balance of nature.  An animal will prey upon another animal because it is natural- it will kill its food using only the tools on its body or those offered by its natural habitat.  If you find an animal you wish to eat, you may kill it for food, but do not make weapons where they don't exist in nature.  You may use only nature and your body.

Kane:  But he carries a knife.

Leon:  He doesn't use it.

Kane:  Then why-

Leon:  No questions about that knife!

Kane gulped, startled by Leon's severity.

Kane (a pause):  Can I ask how you killed the cormorant?

Leon (laughing):  I'm glad you awoke on a cormorant night.

Leon made a throwing motion with his wrist, tossing a loop of beard like a lasso.

Leon:  He throws stones.

Kane:  He throws stones?

Alma:  He can do with one hand what you can't do with two.

Leon:  As demonstrated with the cormorant, if you want to eat a kill, you do it naturally.  You can use fire, as fire may be found in nature, but you may not use unusual utensils to season or cook the meat in a specialized manner.

Kane:  He really throws stones?

Alma:  He didn't always- only after he lost the arm.

Alma blushed, embarrassed.  She excused herself from their discussion, presumably to use the fields.

Leon:  Alma must feel at home with you- she's usually very sensitive to matters that we don't discuss.

Kane nodded.

Leon:  Heart of the People loved the land as he loved music.  He believed that the land was made of music- music was the only element.  This was the premiere principle behind the earthen huts.  He had very progressive ideas, but he didn't recognize how dangerous these ideas could be if they were mishandled or misunderstood.

Kane:  How so?

Leon:  Many members of the tribe, as with any sample of humanity, weren't intellectually capable of grasping his ideas as he meant them.  As you mentioned before, genius must be translated.

Kane:  What was the tribe called?

Leon:  Heart of the People does not believe in names, Kane.

Kane:  So why does he call himself Heart of the People?

Leon:  He doesn't know we call him that.

Kane relaxed under the salted sky, trying to breathe it all in.

Leon:  This idea of the land bothered me for some time.  I felt that if the land was home to us all, if the land was as beautiful as music, then the land shouldn't treat people differently.

Kane's younger self would have pounced on this idea, paralyzing it with an almighty shock, relishing in the final gasps of breath before he killed it.  The tattooed, nomadic Kane Hailand could hardly comprehend.

Kane:  Differently how?

Leon:  South America is considered to be of lesser economic status than Europe.  Civilization didn't develop as rapidly in the Southern hemisphere because the Incas had difficulty with land- this is why we see the acacias built stepwise into the Andes, as crops can only grow on flatland.  This land that you and I now occupy, on the other hand, was called the Fertile Crescent.  Middle Easterners developed their civilization several millennia before the Incas, and the threads of their civilization were manifested in Ancient Greece.  Europe had horses, South America had llamas- the rest, as they say, is history.

Kane:  Okay.

Leon:  Our tribe was composed of a vast variety of peoples from around the world, but all of these people had different histories, different ways of conceptualizing the world.  Now is the time to rise, Heart of the People said, when the people are looking for something new.  Heart of the People was not interested in revolution in the sense of uprooting the government- he was interested in revolutionizing thought.  Convincing people that they needed change was our greatest challenge, he said.  In his mind, his greatest challenge had already been conquered.  I can't blame him for his obstinacy- he once served a woman who required all heads to be delivered on a pike.  Besides, I adored Heart of the People.  Even if a philosophy is blatantly erroneous, and his definitely was not, the ability to think for one's self and escape the maxims one was preached as a child is a sign of greater thinking.

Kane nodded, silently concerned with Alma's absence.

Leon:  We have always hoped for the day when we can put his teachings into practice.  As time progresses, our prospects grow dimmer.  You've seen the leopard's fang around his neck?

Kane:  I was curious about that.

Leon:  He issued an assignment to the tribe- each member was to use music as a tool of giving.  We were to give a gift to a fellow tribesman, incorporating our music.  It was the most wonderful homework I was ever assigned, the type of assignment I would fantasize about as a boy in the almond tree.  I wandered into the desert land, playing my ney as I walked alone, at peace with all of nature.  I noticed a strange train of insects, scorpions, and snakes following me as some sort of animalian pied piper.

Kane Hailand, who once wore the multicolored Jester's hat, who once possessed a magic gift for song, had always considered himself to be a pied piper of sorts.

Leon:  These disease-ridden creatures were following fast, chasing the scent of my blood, and I was petrified.  I walked at an accelerated pace, my mouth always to the ney.  Without my ney, I don't know that I would have escaped the chase without suffering cardiac arrest.  A leopard sauntered forth as if from nowhere.  I clutched my ney tightly, sure that I was dead, a trail of miniature beasts behind me.  The leopard approached.

Kane relaxed.  Leon seemed to think that his story with the animals was extraordinary, but Kane remained unimpressed.  Somehow Kane already knew how the story ended.

Leon:  The leopard was calm, not at all threatening, and I found comfort in its eyes, whistling my ney.  The leopard and I halted, separated by a pace at most, understanding through our eyes that we trusted each other.  And so I played my ney, knowing that my leopard would protect me from the vermin close behind.  The leopard yawned and rested in the sand at my feet, slumped in peaceful sleep in seconds.  The creatures behind had fallen asleep as well- I charmed them with my music.  I basked in the warmth of the greatest sensation a mortal man can be blessed with, elating through my ney.  Though I had previously been awarded international prizes for my literary and scientific work, this was my introduction to pride- glory in the sandy jungle.  I was the hub among myriad creatures swaying in peaceful dreams to my ney.  This was my own gift to me, and I was determined to bring my gift to Heart of the People as well.  Prying the leopard's sleeping jaws, I wrapped my beard around its fang using an advanced application of knot theory.  I pulled the fang with very little force, as my bearded knot was flawlessly tied.  The leopard never woke.  I returned home and presented the leopard's fang to Heart of the People.  He had given us strict instructions that he was to receive no gifts- the assignment was designed for the tribesmen to give to each other.  Besides, Heart of the People accepts no alms.  Upon my presentation, his eyes flashed red.  He couldn't resist the fang.  That fang remains the token of his greatest success, his tutelage of a man who stopped the world with music.  I suppose I have lived many lifetimes.

Kane:  As have I.

Leon:  It's strange- I've since seen this same one-fanged leopard in the Middle East.  It has acquired a limp.  This may sound psychotic, Kane, but I think it may be following me with hungry revenge on its breath.

Kane:  The leopard is a noble creature- far nobler than man, who holds grudges.

An ibex approached, great horns coiled in arches behind its head.  The ibex hoofed forward, its white beard bobbing in the black night sand.

Leon shooed the ibex.

Caped in Alma's hand-woven quilt, Kane rose from his rock and gestured toward the ibex.

Kane (stroking the ibex between the horns):  Do we have any water left?

Leon:  You want to feed the animal?

Kane:  He's thirsty- he must've been separated from his herd.

A herd of about fifteen ibexes scaled the hillside from the river, trotting toward the fire.

Kane:  I'm sorry if they bother you.  Animals have always liked me.

Leon:  My Lord- you too?

Kane:  I tried to tell you- it's a power.

Leon:  How was I to believe that someone else could possess this strange attraction?

Kane kissed the ibexes on the head as they ambled past him in a concatenation of hooves.  Leon froze in shock.

Leon:  I can't believe this.  They come to you without a ney.

Kane's scabbed forehead burned its regret-laden incense of Anika's ash- listening to Leon's story, he was able to forget the inherent sin of The Scoundrels, and it was pleasant.

Kane:  Do you ever get tired of moving?

Leon:  I am a child of the earth, what else am I to do?

Kane:  You're a far cry from the kid who stashed a ney in the almond orchard.

Leon:  The early days in Sudan were perhaps the greatest of my life.  I had my tutor, and he was happy, and everything was right.

Kane:  It doesn't seem so bad out here now.

Leon:  No, I am very fortunate to be living as I am.  I am content with my current direction, and so I cannot be remorseful of the past that led me here.  Heart of the People cannot say the same.  He's not as he once was- something changed him, Kane.  The arm, of course, but there's more to it than that.  We, too, have done the unspeakable.  There's blood on our hands.  I can say no more except for this- I live for day and dread the night, for every time I see that moon and smell its haunting glow, I know my slumbers will forever be intruded by the ghosts of past, fleeing from the stars to ensure that I may share their fate and never learn of peace.

Kane:  You don't sleep?

Leon:  I battle sleep until it inevitably dominates me- this way, when the sleep finally overcomes, it is a dreamless crash.

Kane:  Why don't you want to dream?

Leon:  Dreams give rise to the past in mutated forms.  Certain events from my past, even when doctored to their most attractive form, are incomprehensibly sickening to me.

Leon paused and drew a large breath of air.  He absent-mindedly twisted his large loops of beard between his weathered fingers, blinking hard to fight his past mistakes from leaking out his eyes.

Leon:  Often times, when a man holds a position of power or influence, he forgets his responsibility to act on behalf of all those who place faith in him.  Heart of the People forgot this just once.

Leon paused, sipping water from the pail.

Leon:  A group of armed militiamen drove through our village, riding 4 x 4 vehicles equipped with machine guns.  They shot weapons at our children and our animals.  They set torches to our huts.  I watched all that I held sacred wither and die under flame, the crackling smoke barely audible under the shouts of soldiers strapped with copper-bullet belts.  I watched my brothers die in the rattle of gunfire.  The soldiers debased everything in their path, pillaging their way to Heart of the People.  Our leader rose from his throne, emerging from his earthen hut, bathed in dirt, eyes glaring red.  The army claimed to be sent by the Ministry of Defense.  They read a message from the Minister- allegedly, our way of life defied the vision of the great prophet Elijah Mohammed.  Heart of the People demanded that we had basic human rights.  The head cavalryman laughed.  He aimed his assault rifle at the spot between Heart of the People's red eyes.  I stood beside our tribesmen, rifles aimed at our chests.  We watched the military commander approach our leader.  Heart of the People never flinched.  The commandant stopped when the tip of his rifle was nearly touching our percussionist's skin.  But Heart of the People was not frightened.  Rights? the commandant said, the only right swine has is death.  He unfolded a spike bayonet from the barrel.  This, the commandant barked, is called a pig sticker, and I use it to stick swine.  We watched him lower the bayonet and stab Heart of the People in the thigh.

Kane:  The government did this?

Leon:  A government governs- there's no stipulation in the definition that states a government must govern for the good.

Kane:  What did you do?

Leon:  I prayed.  For the first time in my life, I really prayed.  As a child, my father would harangue me on the significance of prayer, but I only ever prayed to appease him- I never believed in the words I was reciting.  The day I pulled the leopard's fang I felt the presence of God within me, and I knew that He would follow me.  I always thought Heart of the People to be impenetrable.  To see his skin torn as mine could be torn, to see him bleed as I could bleed, annihilated all hope.  And so I prayed.

Leon began sobbing.

Kane:  You don't have to say it.

Leon (wiping his sleeve over his eyes):  It's important that you know.  When faced with the decision of death or imprisonment, death was the more considerate option.  I had read about their prisons- hundreds of men plucked from their families and stuffed into small cells with hundreds of others to inhale diseases and fight for food.  I opted for death.  Heart of the People didn't opt for death- he opted to fight.

Kane:  Did you have weapons?

Leon:  We had Heart of the People.  Now, Kane- you were struck by lightning and you survived.  I find it curious that you have the ability to control all light.

Kane lowered his head, avoiding his own silhouette in the fire.  He was still unaccustomed to having a shadow- he felt strange, as if he was either following something or it was following him.

Kane:  Not anymore.

Leon:  I once had a similar ability.  I intentionally suppressed this gift because I was afraid of what the Bonabali government would coerce me into doing if they discovered my control over energy.  I don't know if you noticed what Heart of the People did during supper tonight, but he has a gift- he has complete control of fire.

Kane:  Like Hank- Hank controls fire.

Leon:  Heart of the People sparked fire to their vehicles.  It was like a great bomb had detonated under the feet of our enemies.  Our leader imploded all but one militiaman.  With just one infantryman to challenge our whole tribe, the battle was over.  The fight was fast and it was bloody and the damaged pieces were irreparable, but it was over- the call to violence had wailed its cry.

Leon broke, sobbing with his head in his hands.  He wished to tell the story beautifully, with elegance and woe as Heart of the People used to tell it, but he couldn't speak.  To speak the story was to revive it, and Leon couldn't bear the horror.  If he could have spoken, he would have infused the story with life.  The surviving members of our tribe circled the lone soldier.  My friends were bloody and wounded and grieving, surrounding the fallen enemy.  Circled round this man, the only breathing remnant from the destruction that had killed our family, the destruction that had killed us all in many ways which we had yet to realize, looking at each other, with our captor now our victim, we felt nothing but uncertainty.  I spotted a crescent-shaped stone at my feet.  I stooped and lifted the stone.  My tribal brothers watched me, impatient to see what I would do next.  I hurled the stone at the enemy.  My brothers followed.  We threw stones- we threw stones for every injustice that we ever stomached, for every privilege that we never saw, for every unanswered frustration, for every right withheld, for every moment throughout history in which one man has been dominated by another, for the stone mountain of everlasting hatred, for every hut we lost, for every child we would never see grow old, for every wound, for every squandered hope, for every misspent promise, for every human storm, for every gun, for every round, for every bullet, for every pond of blood, for every bomb spraying shrapnel and raining liquid metal on our homes, for every stream of smoke in the wheezing sky that rocketed our babies' ashes to the heavens, for every stone that could not be thrown, we regurgitated our stones to pile on the grey stony hatred while somewhere in the world people drummed their drums, plucked slave songs on their strings, blew their horns to pipe sodality, and rung their reeds to sing the song of brotherhood as we only could have done instead of staging this production of weak venomous hate, because elsewhere there were crows that cawed, snakes that hissed, and bugs that buzzed in a futile attempt to remind me that I could have played my ney  to sing of peace, but I instead continued pelting stones with those I loved until the object of our hatred was buried and forgotten, disfigured in raw bruises, broken bones, and bloody hair, no longer human- we threw stones.  But Leon couldn't speak.  All Leon could do was sob.  Gaining composure, he skipped the story and went straight to the lesson that he learned.

Leon:  We were worse than them.  Always, we lived with the harmonious mindset of We, but we were nave in forgetting about Them.  The concept of peace implodes when We are not one with Them.  In peace, there is only a We.  We claimed to believe in music as a guiding force, and then we played the symphony of death.  Violence is the weapon of the weak- there could be no more.  Our tribesmen hung their heads and disbanded in a sorrowful exodus.  I watched my leader sulk in the opposite direction, wallowing away from the burning pits of his once-grand vision, having deserted his drum.  He was still a chieftain to me, and so I followed at a distance, communing with the shadows to conceal my presence, navigating at night.  The events of that night were as ungodly as what the day had brought, and I was coerced to emerge from the shadows to save his life.

Kane:  Save his life from what?

Leon:  That's another story for another time.  We wandered the land together.  We were alive, but the dream was dead under a pile of rocks in the blood that stains a uniform.  Music can no longer bring us simple joy- it's even more powerful to us now than it was before.  We derive our simple joys from the land, as when I showed Heart of the People his first sunflower.  We have vowed not to recruit anyone who fails to find his Anima.  As you've learned, an open recruit policy resulted in the worst hellfire of our lives.  You may join us on one condition- you must prove you know.

Kane:  What do I know?

Leon:  As of now, you know nothing.

Kane:  What will I learn?

Leon:  Surely you have seen your guitar.

Kane:  My guitar?

Leon:  Yes.  It is yours.  He made it for you.  We only play hand-crafted instruments- this is in the code.

Kane:  I don't know what to say- thank you so much.

Leon:  Never thank us.  One day we may have to leave you behind.  You won't be so thankful if that happens.

Kane Hailand felt a windy sweep of change flutter through his being, and suddenly everything was strangely altered from the role it habited just moments ago.  Maybe the change was in the black air of the night- maybe the change was in Kane.

Leon:  You have until the winter solstice.  That only gives you half a year.  If you haven't gained complete mastery over your voice by this time, we must leave you with whatever knowledge you've acquired.  Heart of the People has crafted you a beautiful instrument.  Kane, look at me.

Kane examined Leon's eyes.  These two men sat alone in silent nature, trading stories from the archives of the human tragedy, occluded from prosperity by tales of past, getting by.  Leon's loopy beard danced as he spoke.

Leon (pointing to the guitar):  You don't learn on that.

Leon manipulated his fingers like a spider weaving a web in the air.

Leon:  You learn on this.  You may attempt a note only when you are absolutely certain of your preparedness.  Heart of the People will be displeased if he senses that you are tinkering prematurely.  Tomorrow we set forth.  We must reach fresh water before the next lunar cycle.  There we can bathe.  This is how we migrate.

Kane:  What happened that night- when you saved his life.  It's when he lost his arm.

Leon (rising, moving toward the tent):  That's enough for tonight.  You may use the guitar as a pillow.  Try to get some sleep.

Kane:  I can't believe the man in the story is the same man I met tonight- he spit on my forehead.

Leon turned from the fire and headed into the starlit valley.

Kane:  Leon.

Leon turned back to Kane.

Kane:  What are my chances?

Leon:  Most people don't even try- they simply thank us for our hospitality and go on their way.  What are your intentions?

Kane:  I'd like to learn from you.

Leon:  We've had many neophytes.  Alma is the only one who ever found her Anima.  We met her in the early spring, which gave her about three months longer than the calendar available to you.  I'll be honest with you, Kane- there's something about you that makes me believe.  I have hope that you can save us as we can save you.

Kane:  How long has Alma been with you?

Leon:  Quite a while.  She remembers me before my Leon beard- I imagine it's been over a decade.

Kane:  I'm not right in the head.  I fear I may be dangerous.

Leon:  You don't mean that.

Leon pointed to Kane's tattoo.

Leon:  As a linguist, I'm sure you know the double-meaning of tattoo.

Kane:  I'm afraid I'm not much of a linguist anymore.

Leon:  A tattoo is a rapid, rhythmic drumming.

Before departing, Leon taught Kane to brush his teeth with a rag, using a toothpaste Heart of the People prepared from burnt egg shells, water, seaweed, almond oil acquired from a trader, salt, and the ashes of camel hooves.  After his lesson, Leon walked away.  Again he stopped, turning back to Kane.

Leon:  Say Kane- can I ask you something?

Kane looked up.

Leon:  Do you want Hank to fail?

Kane sipped a moment's worth of thought from the lukewarm pail.

Kane:  Nothing would please me more.

Leon:  We were once a world of fools acting like men, but now we're men who act like fools.  You and Hank once shared the most beautiful experience in either of your lives, and still you quarrel.  I've been to many places and I've seen many sights, but I have never seen peace- may this be the greatest test of faith you ever know.

Leon walked into the black night.  Kane Hailand laid his head on his gracious guitar and tucked himself beneath Alma's blanket in the frivolous valley night.  The guitar made a more comfortable pillow than he remembered.  He was familiar with the concept of sleeping on an instrument- Hank Adaline slept with his head on a plug-in piano for as long as Kane had known him.  Even the fans knew Hank's bed-ware flustered Anika to the point that it was comical to everyone but Kane.  Now Hank slept on a flat red turntable, the record clamp snugly in his earhole.  Kane thought it strange that Heart of the People should choose a guitar for him to play- Kane had always felt an inclination toward the six-stringed wonder.  Given Heart of the People's background, it was odd that he would even know what a guitar was- the fact that he knew how to build one from the parts of nature was even more bizarre.  Leon filled the sky with the spritely tunes of his ney.  Music, to Kane Hailand, was the essence of his war with Hank.  Music, life, was war.  For the first time, Kane Hailand fell asleep to gorgeous music, gazing into the speckled stars, wearing his sneakers and his undershirt under Alma's hand-sewn quilt, drifting into wonderful sleep as he listened to the lullabies of Leon and Alma- Leon who played his ney at night because he was frightened of his ghost-laden dreams, and Alma who was vomiting in the sand.


* * *



Track 4: A Melody For Alma


Upon the wake of dawn, Kane felt the bowels clench his insides.  In painful sickness, he called for Leon, who had passed the sleepless night with his ney.   Leon instructed Kane to use the fields beyond Heart of the People's tent.

Kane:  What do you use for toilet paper?

Leon:  Walk it off- let it air-dry.

Kane's eyes grew large.

Leon:  You use what you can find- stones, leaves, grass.  Heart of the People prefers corncobs, but he'll never share his precious cobs with you.

Kane took a circuitous route to the fields so as not to disturb the sleeping drummer with his footsteps.

Upon Heart of the People's wake, the group traveled with the sun behind them.  Heart of the People navigated, walking barefoot, beating his drum, the contraption on his stump communicating with the land.  Leon tooted his ney while Alma strummed her lyre.  They walked.  The naked earth, that sweet foundation of all life, spun with delight to sing its succor to their humble feet.

Alma told Kane that Leon's ney skills are superlative- he can play chromatically.  A chromatic musical scale, she explained, would include the notes of both the white keys and the black keys on a piano, whereas a diatonic scale consists of the notes from white keys only.  Kane failed to understand the reference, as every key on Hank Adaline's piano was red, but Alma insisted that only the greatest ney players ever harnessed the ability to play chromatically.  Leon could solo in semitones which Alma's lyre could never produce.  Semitones, she explained, were the pitches that lie between the notes on a piano.  Kane could achieve semitones on a guitar by bending the string up or down.  This concept was familiar to Kane- years back, Hank built a semitonal piano, as he believed eighty-eight keys to be limiting.

Heart of the People assigned Kane the responsibility of pulling the wagon.  The wagon, a robust box of four-wheeled wood containing supplies and provisions, weighed nearly five times Kane Hailand's reduced body weight.  Kane was forbidden from loading the wagon, but he watched with furtive acuity as Leon and his leader piled pails of food, instruments, quilts, wood, stones, knives, reinforcement parts for instruments, a barrel of date wine, and a rolled tent into the rectangular space.  Most fascinating, however, was their transportation of water- Heart of the People utilized his absolute control of fire to produce a flame that flickered at subfreezing temperatures, solidifying pails of water into ice, thus facilitating transport.  The freezing flame, Leon later explained, was impossible according to the laws of nature, in which cold flames are a rare phenomenon that burn at a minimum of 120 degrees celsius- Heart of the People created his own laws.

Kane pulled the wagon as he reveled in their canorous music.  He watched Alma strum her lyre, constructed from a hallowed tortoise shell that served as a reverberating soundbox.  She used the horns of a great ram, bolted together at the tips with a wooden crossbar, to act as the lyre's arms.  Wood, she says, would allow for a better sound than the ram's horn, but the ram's horn is more consistent with what the Ancient Greeks played.  An animal skin was pulled tautly over the open side of the shell to fortify the sound, singing from a choir of seven juxtaposed strings running from the tail end of the shell to the crossbar.  The strings, Alma says, were made from catgut- animal intestines drawn, cleaned, scraped, soaked, then burnt.  Alma says the best strings come from goats and sheep, as they have leaner entrails.  Her one hand silenced select strings while her other hand strummed a plectrum chipped from a swan's skull across the open notes.  This music, only angels play.

Though Kane was still prohibited from plucking a guitar string, he was required to hold the guitar as he walked the wagon through dry rivers and cracked sand, simulating finger-patterns into the foreign fretboard, stumbling through the motions.  This exercise seemed pointless to Kane- he considered it impossible to learn an instrument if he was forbidden from hearing its sound.

Never to navigate at night, the vagabonds walked until the great sun descended.  The group stopped just three times, crushing ice fragments into water.  After their first stop, Leon guided Alma by the hand.  Kane wondered curiously why Alma should need Leon's guidance when she appeared to be so independent, but he asked this question to no one but himself.

Sweating to the point of weakness, sucking thin breaths of the desert heat, veins pulsing rhythmically with the chugging tune of his heart as he pulled the wagon up the mountain until sundown, Kane found no comfort under the sun.  He used to relate to the sun, he was the sun- Kane Hailand was once God.  Hank Adaline, the moon.  But even that moon, the mere suggestive hint of the sun's reflection, aggravated Kane's sensitivity to the black desert air.  Through the day's walk, Heart of the People and Alma had only scavenged enough food for three.  Heart of the People endured without.  Kane offered to fast, though he was still quite ill from his misguided peregrination and his strenuous day of wagon-dragging.  Leon refused to translate Kane's fasting offer to Heart of the People, asserting that Heart of the People would lash in anger at the request- the chieftain accepted no alms.  It was a leader's duty to provide for his people, to exalt in their pleasures and to suffer in their stead.  Heart of the People carried the sins of his men across the land, and for his people he will go hungry.  Kane ate his meal in guilty discomfort as those flashing red eyes glared into him with tense fury.

Kane awoke early next morning under Alma's hand-sewn quilt.  Soreness plagued every muscle, filling him with dread for another day of pulling the wagon.

Alma's quilt was patterned with an array of patches, all solid-colored but one.  The lone patch that could boast of any design rested in the upper corner of the quilt, as if she began her blanket with a vast vision of sequence, but became quickly overwhelmed after just one design.  The patch depicted a woman cradling her newborn babe, over an epaulet that read You Make Me Feel Young Again.

Kane lifted his head from the guitar and examined the instrument, wondering how Heart of the People could have made this exquisite jewel with only nature's tools.  He practiced finger-patterns, driving the strings hard into the fretboard because when he pushed the strings harder he could hear the prenatal anticipation of a note.  You don't learn with the guitar, you learn with the air that you breathe- what could Leon have meant?  The prospect of mastering this alien apparatus in the next four lunar cycles became at once impossible and terrifying.  Kane had the fortune to spend years closely examining a master at work with his instrument.  Every time Hank Adaline sat at a piano it was a merging of beings- it wasn't the red piano singing, it was Hank's voice plugged through ten fingers.  Kane Hailand never had the capacity to forge a relationship like that with a lifeless piece of wood.  He had to try- he realized in the pith of his being that these three strange drifters were granting him an opportunity to do something he always felt capable of achieving, yet had always been too scared to attempt.
