For too long, he had denied his self.  He could no longer bear the suspense- it was time to play, time to become.

Kane wandered several hundred yards into the fields, until he was sure that no one would hear him.  He placed his index finger and his pinky over the first and third fret of the top string, and plucked with his thumb.  The note deepened when he lifted his pinky.  As he dragged his index finger closer to the body of the guitar, the instrument produced a higher note.  Kane duplicated the process on the second string, estimating the notes on this string to be higher than those of the top string.  Thus, Kane Hailand explored the guitar.  The fretboard is a world in which man's finger can travel as man travels over his own world of land.  Kane's index finger jumped from fret to fret, string to string, driving into the violent wood with no destination, having no idea where it was or the name of the note it was producing, somehow always knowing what sound will come next.  He introduced his middle finger, playing multiple strings at once.  His playing would have sounded cacophonous to an outsider, but to Kane Hailand it was a celebrated concerto.  He lumbered on, picking with his thumb, eyes closed in a world of sound to block the sun he so reviled, playing and exploring, playing and exploring, until a hand palmed the crown of his head.

Kane squinted to see beautiful Alma in the sun.

Kane:  I-

Alma:  I won't tell him, Kane.

Kane (bowing without knowing why):  Thank you.

Alma:  Come back.  He'll be awake soon.

Alma lifted her palm from the crown of Kane's head.  She turned back for the campsite.  There was an understanding with Alma that Kane had never felt with another human, the sort of reciprocation he always desired with Anika.  Suddenly, he ached for Alma's gentle palm to grace his head yet again, yearning for her sweet hand as if her palm atop his head would unlock a portal to light the world in everlasting harmony.

Kane returned to camp just before Heart of the People emerged from his tent to spew his foamy toothpaste into the sand and load the wagon.  Sitting on a rock, guitar in lap, Kane watched Heart of the People move, barefoot in a musical silence, his stump-shoulder swaying to a beat in his head.  Somehow the stump seemed preferable to an arm, as if it was only natural that the stump was cut short of the elbow, and even if there were a hand below it would be nothing but a bony growth.  Heart of the People allowed only Leon to handle his belongings.  Why, if Kane was to pull the wagon, was he not allowed to load it?  And if Alma had earned her Anima, then why couldn't she load the wagon?  The one-armed percussionist kept a blade in a leather holster at his pantline, just below the stump.  Kane knew, based on the items he witnessed loaded onto the wagon, that the man possessed a vast collection of wooden knives, yet this particular knife never left the holster.  This man was Kane's leader, his navigator and provider, who acknowledged Kane's existence just once to spit on his violet tattooed forehead.  The barefoot, one-armed, knife-toting preacher with a leopard's fang necklace- Heart of the People's enigma was Kane's fiery intrigue.

Leon approached, his two rings of braided beard dancing beneath his arms.  A momentary flare in Leon's brow unnerved Kane to think that maybe Leon knew of his dalliance with the guitar in the field, but this paranoia disappeared with a beam of Leon's friendly bearded smile.

Leon:  Lead us away from here, wagon-boy.

Kane rose and walked with Leon toward the wagon.  The band journeyed on, passing through the dirt mountains under the tramontane sun in pursuit of a farmer's tent.  Kane asked Leon how it could be that Heart of the People knew his way to the farmer's abode, navigating with no map or compass in this unmarked land.  The response he received was simple- the land needs no signs or language, the land has its own markings.  There is no language in nature.  There is only music.

Heart of the People stopped the wagon just once along the way, listening to a birdsong as he drew one of his wooden knives from the bed of the wagon to pick the ice.  Each of the journeyers were given a sizable chunk of ice to suck, which proved difficult for Kane because he had to hold the icicle while pulling the wagon with a guitar strung over his shoulder.  Always, there was that blade winking at Heart of the People's hip.  No longer able to bear the curiosity, Kane caught up to Leon.

Kane (whispering):  Why does he carry that knife?  He never uses it.

Leon (a crunch of ice behind his loopy beard):  You have secrets- so does he.

They reached the farmer's tent by sundown.  Leon stopped Kane to show him a circled X carved into the farmer's wooden camel pen.  This circled X, Leon said, meant that the man who lived here would be kind to traveling strangers.  There are many signs of this sort, all of which would be beneficial for Kane to know.  This sign was what originally led the group to Moishe's friendship years ago.

Heart of the People greeted Moishe with a one-armed embrace.  They shared voices, speaking what Kane could only assume to be words.  Kane was ordered to shut his eyes while the percussionist enacted his astounding creation of fire.  Heart of the People uttered to Leon, who translated to Kane that he was to fetch two pails of ice from the wagon, as the shepherd's camels were thirsty.  Kane, who was nearing complete exhaustion from another day of dragging the wagon, dreaded this last little chore.  Though he was weak, he lumbered back toward the wagon to transport the ice, feeling somehow valuable for the first time since The Scoundrels disbanded.

The farmer's tent was modest, cut in the sand- no walls, just a beige tarpaulin roof structured over several beams, secured by string in the sand.  The inside was decorated with cookware, a single kerosene lamp, and copious rugs that the farmer traded in a marketplace several miles beyond the dusthill terrain where a semblance of civilization allegedly flourished.  According to Leon, Moishe occupied the one fertile patch of land in the valley, and his family had lived on this plot for nearly seven hundred years.  Many raiders have tried to farm this land, but Moishe's family secret has proven to be the only effective means of harvesting.

Moishe had three wives- Olsa, Hilsa, and Yelsa.  He produced one daughter with each wife- Elsa, Alsa, and Hen, all three of whom had their fingertips dyed red with henna.  The three wives invited their guests inside, entreating the band to sit cross-legged on the rugs.  Sheltered by the shield of tent against the moon and stars after one of the few days in his life in which he ever really committed to work, Kane Hailand was never more satisfied.  The farmer's youngest daughter distributed a plate of beans and rice to Kane, Leon, and Heart of the People.  Alma's plate contained no beans.

Kane:  Why didn't you get beans?  Is it a gender thing?

Alma:  I don't eat beans, Kane.  In Ancient Greece, it was thought that beans contain the souls of the dead.

Leon (chewing his beans):  As you can see, I've successfully brainwashed Alma into thinking she's in Ancient Greece.

Alma:  My advice to you, Kane, is to believe the opposite of whatever Leon says.  The shame of his brilliance is that everything he does is part of some ruse far greater than the rest of us are capable of conceiving.

Leon:  To you, it appears to be a shame- to the world it shall be a blessing.

Leon raised his cup toward Heart of the People.  He spoke in Arabic.  Heart of the People gave a solemn nod and downed his wine.

Alma:  The truth is, Kane, I've always been fascinated with Ancient Greek culture.

The farmer's eldest daughter halved two apples and granted a half to each of the guests.  Heart of the People handed his halved apple to Alma, who took a knife to the skin.

Kane:  What are you doing?

Alma:  Paring the apple.

Leon:  How do you pear an apple?

Alma:  He can't eat the skin- it's bad for his teeth, bad for his playing.

Alma passed the naked apple back to Heart of the People, who devoured the pale fruit in several heartbeats.

Moishe wore a headdress and a smile as he smoked a narrow pipe, lines cut into his face to form an aureole around his eyes, relaying an impression of permanent happiness.  His wives and daughters served dinner while he smiled and puffed hashish, speaking through browned teeth.  In some sick way, all of this made Kane think of Anika.

Kane:  What's he smoking?

Leon:  He knows only one phrase in English- watch this.

Leon uttered to the farmer, who laughed in Kane's direction.

Moishe:  Dis sum good sheet.

Leon roared with laughter.  The farmer laughed back.

Leon:  Have a puff, Kane.

Kane:  I gave up substances a long time ago.

Moishe passed his pipe to Heart of the People, who toked deeply and puffed rings of smoke.  He passed to Leon.  Feeling high, Leon passed the pipe to Alma.  Immediately, a white leap of shock jumped to the flesh above his beard.

Leon:  I'm so sorry.

Alma (blushing):  It's okay.  Don't feel bad.

Moishe unbolted a wooden chest and removed a thin stringed instrument.  An odd look, suggestive of a smile but not quite a smirk, overtook Heart of the People's stoned face, his red eyes flashing like lanterns.

Leon:  That instrument is called a rebab- this man plays the finest rebab in the Middle East.

Kane:  You don't say.

Leon:  Kane, you're a performer?

Kane:  Yes.

Leon:  Our host loves to go to performances.  He watches performers build the suspense of their act and then he leaves just before the trick- that way every performance is the best he's ever seen.

Heart of the People bit into a fleshy mango, leaving a trail of taste down his tongue.  He rinsed his mouth of the mango's sugar and spit into the sand.  Then he took another hit from the pipe.

Kane:  If he's supposed to be taking such good care of his teeth, why does he smoke?

Leon (ignoring Kane):  You know, Kane, my father would have scoffed at Moishe.  Our humble host is a being who has no goals, no drive, and no determination.  To him, stress is an unknown sickness.  Nobody will remember his existence when he dies, a concept he is unconcerned with.  He doesn't understand the concept of remembrance because he has never truly feared his own death- he's one of the few men I've met who has ever really known happiness.

Leon retrieved his ney, Alma her lyre, Heart of the People his talking drum.  Kane transcended in their unnamable harmony with a biting envy, hoping to one day join their likes.  When alas the band had ceased to play, Heart of the People muttered to Leon.  Leon translated to Kane, who was still too dazed from the music to completely register what Leon was saying.

Leon:  Tomorrow, Kane, you are to scavenge with Alma.

Kane Hailand slept in the desert to the sweet sounds of music, snoring in a tent atop the global antipode of Hank Adaline, who sat at a red piano in his red private jet, en route to the premiere of his solo world tour.


Alma woke Kane with a lean caress of moisturizer to his scabbed forehead.  Ever annoyed with the dehydrated skin that plagues a nomad, Alma brewed the moisturizer from a mix of water, olive oil, and beeswax that Leon had bravely obtained without succumbing to a single sting.  All pain was freed from the cage of Kane's skull when Alma placed her hand over his scars.

Alma:  I thought we'd get an early start.  Here's your pail.

Kane rose from Alma's quilted blanket.

Alma:  I sheared one of the sheep last night, so I'll have enough wool to finish your sweater.  I'm sorry it took so long- you must be freezing in that undershirt.

Kane:  You've been very kind.  I just- I appreciate all you've done for me.

Alma blushed.  Kane had seen that blush in Anika years ago, before he ruined her- he always understood the blush to mean that there was romance and that it was precious, but a woman like Alma who was seemingly so independent and motherly, so wise in her indeterminate age, couldn't really be interested in a childish game like romance.

Alma:  Let's head east.

Kane:  Why east?

Alma:  We'll follow the sun as it rises.  When at last the sun is overhead, we'll turn back home and follow the sunset.

Kane:  Let's go west- escape the sun.  When the sun catches up, we'll turn back and run the long way home.

Alma smiled in agreement, hair flowing over slender shoulders, a blonde chrysanthemum in her ear.  Kane lifted his empty pail and set forth, wearing beaten sneakers in the sand.

Alma:  Don't forget your guitar.  And grab a pail of ice- we'll need it.

They walked west over the vast landscape, alone with the sun, uniquely empowered.  Many words appeared in Kane's head, but he couldn't combine a sentence that seemed worthy of Alma.  There was a mutual respect between them that he felt should be recognized, but any recognition carried a risk that his perceived compulsions toward their connection might not exist outside of his mind.

Kane:  I mean it.  About the sweater.  I'm really lucky to have found you guys.

Alma:  We're lucky to have found you, Kane.  It could've been anybody lying by the river.

Kane:  You're a great weaver.

Alma:  You haven't seen the sweater yet.  For all you know, it's unsightly.

Kane:  The quilt- the one I've been sleeping with.  It's beautiful.

Alma:  Oh, you're exaggerating.

Kane:  I'm serious.  That one patch, You Make Me Feel Young Again, the mother and her baby- it's very artistic.

Alma:  I didn't make that quilt.  My mum weaved it in the hospital, waiting to give birth to me.

Kane:  So that's you and her in the quilt?

Alma:  Growing up, I always thought so.  The more I think about it, I feel like it's supposed to be me on that patch- me with my baby.

Alma rubbed a hand over her stomach as if a simple rub could impregnate her with her one unattained wish.

Alma:  That blanket is the only thing I have from her.

Kane:  You shouldn't have wasted it on me these last few nights.

Alma:  But you looked so cold.

Kane:  Do you miss her?

Alma:  Who?

Kane:  Your mom.

Alma:  I never knew her.  She died shortly after I was born.  My first day of life was her last.  We shared nothing but mere minutes of our lives, and still the bond that we established breathes inside me and it guides me through my journey.

Kane:  That's beautiful.

Kane spotted greenery in the remote distance.  For once, the landscape didn't seem spoiled.

Alma:  Do you ever feel that there are certain moments that live forever?

Kane:  That's kind of what I wrote about in Train of Power.

Alma:  In Ancient Sparta, the greatest honor a woman could earn was to die during childbirth.  Only women who died during childbirth were given names on their headstones.

Alma pointed left to a copse on the horizon.

Alma:  There's a fig tree in the south.

The only trees Kane saw did not appear to bear fruit.  Regardless, he followed Alma, as she was undoubtedly a more resourceful scavenger than he.  The particular tree she spotted was the lushest among those in its remote copse, bearing dewy bunches of fruit that sprouted between sponge-veined leaves- Alma had a spectacular eye for the lushest trees, yet she seemed doubtful of her visual acumen when teaching Kane how to pick fruit.  She instructed him to pick the figs that were golden yellow.  These were the ripest fruits.  Kane picked the figs and dropped them in his pail as Alma pretended to watch.

Alma:  I'm going to make the most delicious jam from these figs.  Drop some in my pail too, please.

Kane:  I was thinking we put a different fruit in yours- separate the fruits.

Alma:  We can never be certain that we'll find another fruit- and I need to see to it he eats.

Kane:  What do you mean?

Alma:  Heart of the People won't eat any food that you've gathered.  He'll only eat what I've collected.

Kane:  What's wrong with my food?

Alma:  Nothing, Kane, it's just that he doesn't consider you part of the family yet.  You know that.  He doesn't take any alms.

Kane:  Why'd he send me to get food if he won't eat what I bring home?

Alma paused.  She held an open hand toward Kane and swallowed hard.

Kane:  Are you alright?

Alma (gathering herself):  You need to learn how to gather- so you don't cheat death by a river again.  Here, try the date.

Alma fed a date to Kane.  Kane bit into the forever of the fruit and the sweet juice exploded in flavor that delights the sky.  It was then that Kane realized that one could see more in a fruit than people have seen in whole lifetimes.  Fruit is not fruit if it defies its nature, to have its every drop of juice adulated as the holy miracle of life.  Hank Adaline always said that the original sin was not the act of eating, but the nature of eating- sin is not sin if its nature is true.

Kane and Alma fled the sun as they foraged through limestone canyons.  The mountains were high and vast and plenty.  Alma taught Kane that the best method of preserving meat is to slice it thin and preserve it on a rack.  The meat dries, but it stays.

Alma:  Did you know that if the earth's entire existence were minified into a one-year span, people would have only existed for six minutes?

Kane:  I had no idea.

Alma:  Six minutes out a whole year- that's how young we are.  The first homo sapien would be born on December 31st at 11:54 pm.  Yet think about our impact on the world.  We've sculpted nature to our liking- in six relative minutes.

Kane:  Not this place- we haven't even touched this place.

Alma:  That's why this place is the most wonderful.

This was the first time a woman had ever challenged Kane intellectually.  Anika, he always thought, had no intellectual pursuits.  He tried so many times to teach her, to lure her intrigue with everything from Keats to Nietzsche, but she always reverted back to sex.  The truth, however, is that Kane had never given any woman a chance to challenge him intellectually.  He never tolerated a challenge from anyone except Hank Adaline.  Kane Hailand was always the guide in conversation- the ideas of others were simply unimportant.  For the first time, Kane was listening to what others had to offer.  It was as though the act of opening his forehead allowed all acquired knowledge to seep out, and the only way to acquire new wisdom was to listen for it.  Yet no matter what he learned, he was plagued with thoughts of Anika.

Kane:  You still haven't told me about what brought you out here.

Alma:  You still haven't asked.

Kane:  Will you tell me your story?

Alma:  My mum named me Alma, a variation of Gaia, the Great Mother of all gods in Ancient Greek mythology.  My father didn't agree with this name- he wanted a name from the bible.  He was a very pious man.

Alma stopped walking.  She took a moment to breathe the pleasant hum of the still outdoors, gauging her location based on sound because her sight was failing her.

Alma:  She died just minutes after I was born.  I've been told that she tucked me in my quilt at her breast, and whispered You make me feel young again, Alma.  These were her last words before she died with her baby in her arms and a smile on her face, the only interaction we shared.  My father was overwhelmed- he turned to the church, the same place he had always turned to, and still he found no answers.  He never thought to look anywhere else.

Kane:  You've found your answers here.

Alma:  I don't have a fantastic tale like Leon.  Or even you, Kane- your story is wonderful.  I was raised in West Yorkshire.  I was never given many choices.  My dad was afraid to lose me like he lost my mum.  He wouldn't let me do anything that might be dangerous.  I was forced into religion, discouraged from the things I liked to do.  When I grew up, my dad sent me to university so I could study law.  I changed my path of study to history and never told him.  I read law books in my spare time so that I could keep the lie going when he telephoned.  He never learned the truth until graduation.

Kane:  Wow.

Alma:  He was furious, as you can imagine.  He said I couldn't make money with a history degree.  I told him I wasn't concerned with money.  He just called me naive.  I think this story is quite typical, Kane.

Kane:  Keep going.

Alma:  He said the next thing I had to do was to find a man to marry.  I needed a man if I was to live independently- those were his words, all of them senseless.  He lectured me endlessly on choosing a husband.  Of course, I was young and angry, and I ignored almost everything he said- but there was one statement I recall quite clearly.  Choosing a mate is the most important decision one can make, he told me.  He said that I was lucky to live in a time and a place when a mate can be chosen instead of assigned.  He said that his decision to marry my mum, the most important decision of his life, was also his best.  That amazed me.

Kane:  Yes, it's amazing.

Alma:  But it led me to another thought.  I am bred with the choice of whom I wish to marry- I can opt not to marry at all, if I so desire.  The choice to marry a land, however, is concealed.  When you choose a neighborhood as a home, it is a settlement, a marriage.  Why confine myself to just one neighborhood- why marry just one land if I wish to marry them all?

Kane:  Let me think about this.

Alma:  Unlike the decision to marry a man, the decision of whether or not to marry a land is hidden from us, Kane.  They don't want us to know the choice exists because if too many people chose the single life, as we have, then society would crumble.

Kane:  How so?

Alma:  It's the same with genetics- if we don't reproduce, as traditional marriage implies, we collapse as a species.

Kane:  Right.

Alma:  If no one were to commit to a town, the work needed to keep that town afloat would go unfulfilled.  A society is a machine that functions on its workers- without workers, society would collapse.

Kane:  I see.

Alma:  I found the choice and I made it.  I told my dad that I'm not his baby alone- I am a child of this earth.  I couldn't live with the knowledge that I may die without having seen what this good earth has to offer.

Kane:  What'd he say?

Alma:  Nothing.  He prayed to mum.  Then he helped me pack.  He understood.  The last thing he ever said to me was that she would be proud.  I traveled all of Europe, then all of Asia.  After years of travel, I decided to head back home to England.  When I was nearly halfway back, I met Heart of the People.  He was assisting his friend Mountain Song at a local market.  They were selling grains, but I was fixated on the strange instrument that lay upon the sack of birdseed.  I asked him how much for harp.  They didn't understand.  Luckily, Leon overheard, and he interpreted for me.  They said the lyre was mine for free, provided I dine at their house.  I've been with them since.

Kane:  I think your story is beautiful.

Alma:  Throughout history, wise men have devoted their lives to asceticism.  To shed all material desires, they detached from their physical selves and merged with God in nature.  I'm not out here trying to touch some higher calling- it's just the only place I ever felt home.

Kane (pointing into the horizon):  Can we pick from that tree?

Alma squinted, swiveling her head as if to follow a tennis match.  Kane watched this odd cranial motion with amused discomfort as he waited for a response that never came.  Alma adjusted her sandals, which were digging blisters into her ankles, and slid her hand into his palm, nudging against his body, her delicate fingers falling softly in his calloused hands.  Kane understood that he was to guide her by the hand as he had seen Leon do.  There was something about the way she offered her hand- soft, petite, and undeniably feminine in his massive palm- that assured Kane she did not offer her hand to Leon in this manner.  These thoughts distracted Kane from questioning why it was that Alma would need to be guided by the hand.

The pair approached the thicket of trees.  Without investigating the nature of the tree, Alma suggested they take a break under the shade.  She removed her wooden sandals and lay perfectly cocooned inside a groove in the tree-trunk, her flowery scent rising with a faint touch of sycamore bark.  Her poor vision made her dizzy.  Kane sat atop his guitar.  He lifted the pail of ice in his lap, finding a thin film of water melted over the ice.

Kane:  There's some water on the top.  Have some.

Alma thanked him and sipped the water.  She drew a sharpened stick from her satchel and handed it to Kane to chisel the ice.  Alma held a chunk of ice to the blisters at her ankle.

Kane:  Why don't you wear shoes that are more comfortable?

Alma:  I like the way these look.

Kane:  No one's here to see you.

Alma:  You can see me.  But enough about me.  I wanna hear your song Train of Power.

Kane froze.  He came to with his hand unconsciously protecting the photo in his pocket.

Alma:  You still haven't come to peace with that song.

Kane:  It's that obvious?

Alma:  Let me ask you something- did you know what you were doing when you performed that piece?

Kane:  I had absolute control of every moment.

Alma:  What about when you made your other music?

Kane:  I never recaptured the feeling.

Alma:  Yet the world loved your other music.  The music you made when you weren't so sure of yourself was your most successful music.

Kane:  Commercially.

Alma:  I know the feeling you had on Train of Power.  We enjoy this feeling every night because we know the exact purpose of our music.  This is something the world wouldn't appreciate.

Kane:  Why do you think?

Alma:  People don't want Art anymore- they want entertainment.  They want to see a crash of the worst possible sort.

Kane:  My whole life has been a crash.

As a kid, the young Kane was carted about from professor to professor in an effort to tame and quantify his intellect.  Mrs. Whilms, the orphanage administrator, was a great supporter of the young man's achievements.  It was with her help that Kane (or Jake, as he was called) received a full-ride scholarship to Uncle Dross University where he met Hank Adaline and they formed the Violet 9.  Now Kane was living among nomads with a tattoo on his forehead- if Hank Adaline ever learned what had become of Kane, he'd laugh so hard he'd shit.

Kane (offering a fistful of ice chips to Alma):  So, you're really into this Greek stuff.

Alma (through crunches of ice):  My mum was Greek.  But it's more than that- they formed our modern world.  The Greeks were the pioneers who established the way we think.

Kane:  Yes, but there were prior civilizations that influenced the Greeks.

Alma:  Several civilizations far more ancient made tremendous progress, but the Greeks gave birth to genius.  It was the high point of civilization.  I think the world is still chasing something that we lost in their decline.

Kane:  What are we chasing?

Alma:  Our primal source, our beginning.  Society wants to outdo its predecessors like we want to outdo our parents.

Kane:  Do you think it will happen?

Alma:  I think either it will happen or all of humankind will fade.

Kane:  I used to be smart like you.

Alma (brushing her hair behind the flower in her ear):  It made me wonder- if men ignored all subsequent work since the Greeks, since their birth, they could essentially direct their lives in any direction, without the influence of culture.  This is what we do with our music- we invent ourselves from the moment of creation.

Kane sucked at a chunk of ice, alone in the world with this enchantress whose every utterance cleared away bits of the manic psychosis that brought him into her care.  She was intelligent, with a vital sense of music that encompassed the complete emotional spectrum and perhaps some of the supernatural as well.  And she was beautiful.  Her beauty was not immediately apparent to Kane- it appeared in a progressive uncovering as he became gradually reacquainted with virtue.  Her radiant smile, the flower in her hair, her striking dark eyebrows, sweet sapphire eyes, and even the thin penumbra of bristles over her lip- Alma wore white, as Anika never would.  Melodious lyrics materialized in the songwriter's head- wasting lazy time in the shade, where truth is not told it is made.  Every moment under the tree was a blessing of being, laughing with a woman for once in his life.

Kane:  There's a song of mine I think you'd like.  I wrote it for Anika, called Helen of Troy.

Alma:  I wish you'd sing it for me.

Kane:  No one can sing an Anika vocal without shaming it- especially me.  I can't sing.

Alma:  Recite it.

Kane:  Not many people ever appreciated the song- I guess the Face that Launched a Thousand Ships is no longer hip.

Alma:  I've been away a long time, but it doesn't shock me to hear that all the kids aren't raving over Helen of Troy.

Kane:  You don't speak of Rome the way you speak of Greece.

Alma:  Please!  Rome was a cultural parasite to Greece.  They didn't have nearly the impact on art.  And their music was awful.

Kane:  Greek women weren't treated very kindly- they were considered inferior.

Alma:  Now you can begin to understand me.

Alma's smile faded into a look of meek sincerity, which she wiped away with a giggle.  Leaning forward from her nook in the tree, she reached for Kane's hand.

Alma:  Come, we have a lot more scavenging to do.

Kane:  What about this tree?

Kane placed a bunch of berries into Alma's palm.  She held them at a strange angle to the left of her eye and wafted them under her nose before tossing them to the ground.  Watching this strange act, it occurred to Kane for the first time that Alma's vision might be stunted.  She said nothing.  He mimicked her silence.

Kane:  Poison?

Alma:  Maybe.  I'm not sure what fruit this is.  Never eat what you're unsure of.

The guitar strung over Kane's shoulder, two pails in his left hand and Alma in his right, they stepped into the sunlight.

Alma:  You have such large hands.

Kane:  You should see Hank's hands.  His hands are like an alien's.  He always said if his hands were any larger, he could explode the world with sound.  I believe it.  I wish I could explain what a gift he had.  He just stopped playing piano- to DJ.  He used to get so angry at the popularity of electronic music that he'd drug himself into oblivion.  Then he ditched the world's greatest piano chops to DJ.  To give up a gift like the gift Hank has with that piano is a crime against God.

Alma:  People dream of fame and fortune, but look how unhappy it's made you.

Kane:  I'm not as unhappy as you think.

Alma:  I fear that you're worse.  I see it in your face.

Kane:  That's not true.

Alma:  Yes it is.  I've been there.  I grew numb. Kane and Alma reached Moishe's tent as the sun set behind them.  The farmer served Alma's fig jam with a gracious gruel mixed from semolina, honey, and goat cheese.  Adding lemon juice to goat milk, Leon explained, makes goat cheese.  You let the milk coagulate and then you drain it.  Goat cheese is the source of the gruel's salty flavor, as the cheese must be heavily salted to prevent decay.

Leon remarked to Kane that Moishe can't possibly talk about the land because the farmer doesn't recognize the land, just as a fish doesn't recognize the water he swims in.  To him, the land is indistinguishable from the air and the water because they are all a part of him, just as Kane cannot distinguish between different facets of his own bundled personality.  Heart of the People flashed his eyes although he couldn't possibly have known what was said.

Leon related Moishe's gratitude for the rare treat of a hedgehog.  Moishe posited that the only shame of it all was that he couldn't eat the hedgehogs with his friends that very night, as hedgehogs take half an evening to cook, and they're best boiled when wrapped in a certain type of grass that the farmer did not then have in his possession.  Moishe also noticed that Kane had developed a certain fondness for the hedgehogs, and the farmer didn't want his new tattooed American friend to watch his little hedgehog buddies get their throats cut- Moishe killed all of his meals by slitting their throats.

No matter what adversity confronted Kane throughout the day, the prospect of bathing in their music at night endowed him with the strength to persevere. In the morning, Moishe sent them off with a pail of goat milk.  Heart of the People didn't bother to freeze the milk, as it was to be consumed quickly to avoid spoilage. They continued northwest, where they could seek shelter from the impending storm in the fortress of a Bedouin who traded soap- Kane thought this to be emphatically appropriate, as he was beginning to revile his own stench.  Alma somehow defied the logic of the wild, as she had soft skin that smelled like flowers.  Kane pulled the wagon with the guitar strung round his shoulder. Making perfume was a simple process, one that Heart of the People would spurn if he knew she kept a glass vial, because glass defied his code.  Place a woven cloth over a jar and fill it with flowers.  Pour water and alcohol into the jar until it covers the flowers.  Let it sit for several days, filter the product through a woven cloth, and voila- perfume.  Though she embraced the wild, she was still a woman, soon to be the Great Mother of man, and she must carry a woman's scent. Though he hadn't yet spoken, she could see this in his face- he needed her help.  He was just a silly man with a backwards tattoo of some strange expression on his forehead. They had to protect him at all times because thousands of strange animals would come to see his body, as if they could smell his weakness. He needed a mother, to teach and to guide him, and who could mother the man who was to father all men if not she- the great mother, Gaia, Alma? Another urge to vomit.  There's no stopping it- the sickness will come if it wants to.  Breathe.  Exhale.  When she goes to the fields there's blood in her urine and she's too tired to continue walking like this and the moisturizer gives her a rash.  The lyre is the only thing that could alleviate the pain, but that would be too easy. At sundown the family set up camp in the mountains.  Kane watched Heart of the People light a fire, and his suspicions were confirmed- Heart of the People made fire just like Hank Adaline, sparking flames through his palms.  Heart of the People still refused to look at Kane, and even when he did turn to Kane's direction it was as if he was looking through Kane with something that was either utter disinterest or total concentration. Kane sat under the moon, a voyager in the deep unknown of the fretboard.  With acute concentration, he was able to recall the music of The Scoundrels.  Memory was the only union between the tattooed vagabond and the lyricist from the greatest musical trio of all time.  Given his imminent deadline for mastering the guitar, he figured the most efficient method for learning the instrument would be to transcribe The Scoundrels' music.  These were the only songs of which he had any sense of structure, as he meticulously inspected every step of their development from a series of flashing words in his mind to a full-bodied performance in the world's grandest arenas.

The first song he chose to learn was Turkish Bath- four verses portraying an isolated dome where all inhibitions are extinguished upon entry and redressed upon exit.  This was another fan favorite in shows.  When Kane Hailand threw undulating waves of light like an ocean in the air, the audience understood that the next 99 beats played at varying paces contained a universe in which the boundaries of social conventions did not exist.  At the end of the song, when Hank Adaline pulled his red keyboard from the piano and into the audience like a sliced treadmill, every fan would stop to marvel at Hank's globe of fire, suspended in the air.  The audience would wonder for a moment what chaos had just ensued and then they'd turn for their respective homes, leaving their rubble for the stadium crew to clean.  The Scoundrels were required by law to quit performing the song in most countries after a group of fans raped a young girl during a concert at the Sydney Opera House.  The rapists claimed it was consensual, the girl claimed differently- it was an international debate that lasted far too long.  Kane Hailand was devastated- Hank Adaline, that devil, was proud.  Kane originally wrote Turkish Bath to be a song about unattainable love, but the world took it for a song about anarchy- then again, how often in this world is a man properly heard?

Kane hoped Alma would appreciate the song- it wasn't a romantic tune, but it was important for the indications of his former frustrations.  Alma was trying to understand him as Anika never did, but he could still only think about Anika.  Because he left her.  He loved her and he left her when she was dying and now there was no way to contact her even if he wanted to.

While thinking, Kane transcribed, picking four-note chords through trial and error, settling with a composer's certainty.  Kane had already transcribed the first two verses through trial and error when Leon approached.

Leon:  A young guitar is blooming.

Kane:  I hope so.

Leon:  Come with me.  There's something you must do.

Lured by the ranging echo of the talking drum, Leon led Kane to the fire.  Heart of the People approached Kane with a barefoot sweep of his stump, heatedly staring with his red flashing eyes.  The grand leader released the drum-rope from his jaws.  He raised his one arm to rest a hand on Kane's shoulder.

Leon handed Kane a pail with a small volume of mysterious liquid inside.

Leon:  Swirl it around your mouth.  Don't swallow.

Kane obliged, unconscious of his obedience, ricocheting the liquid bullet between his cheeks, through his teeth and under his tongue, a taste of coconut and sesame. After a minute or so, Leon told Kane to spit.  Leon pointed through a loop of beard into the distance.

Leon:  Love and be loved.

Mouth freshened with coconut flavor, Kane walked forth to the beat of the drum and the whistle of the ney, guided by rhythm, living their voice in the chill of the night.  Through the faint flicker of the fire behind him, Alma was wrapped in a blanket beneath the starlit sky.

She was a woman, the only key to his understanding of a greater power.  Affixed to him their bodies shall alas become full, and the high signal of creation will sound through the land.  Her eyes, infinite in depth, penetrated his being- he said nothing, yet she understood all.

Alma:  Kane.

She stepped forth and burrowed her head into his chest.  He pulled her close and he wished that he could somehow pull her closer so as to share her grace and think her every thought and drive her spirit.  His hand cradled the back of her head and it fit so perfectly as if hand and head were crafted in identical molds, yet not like yin and yang because there is no hand inside head or head inside hand, and head and hand were not created equal.  His hand had complete control of her head- he was her guide and she would go wherever his hand willed.  This must be how Hank Adaline felt, sitting at a piano- the ability to master the world with his hand.  Kane pushed Alma's head downward.  Anika always began with her mouth- she always said she needed a warm-up.

Alma:  Kane, what are you doing?

The drum and ney ceased.

Kane:  I'm sorry- I thought-

Alma:  Don't you have any manners?

Leon (shouting from far beyond the fire):  He doesn't have womanners.

Alma (shouting back to Leon):  Shut up.  Just keep playing.

The music continued.  Kane Hailand wallowed in the soil of shame.  He had acted on impulse, as Anika trained him to act.  Anika liked to be ridden and abused. Alma took a moment to gather her composure.  Her first thought was that she had misinterpreted his actions because his personality didn't match the sexual demeanor, but then it made sense that Kane Hailand was fractured in spirit.  He had an exceeding capacity to love, but it had never been requited. Due to silver spots in her vision that she didn't want him or anyone else to know about, lest they pity her more, Alma had not yet produced a complete image of his appearance. Alma:  Where are you going, Kane?

Kane:  I don't know.  That's what scares me.

Kane kissed her slender female shoulder.  She pleaded with him to take her, to treat her as a man treats a woman- to violate and worship her until the final dawn.  This was not a physical act.  This act was not in the body.  Adolescent boys have dreams that wake them up with sticky sheets without as much as a touch of a finger.  Making love is in the mind, in the spirit- it is not as it is portrayed in Hollywood or literature or any other stimulation of the senses.  It is a holy conversation between souls, hearts beating in each other's chests.  Kane felt Alma's warm light in his soul.  Alma felt Kane's warm light in her soul.  Together these two lovers, yin and yang, became whole as one, drifting into the night with shared lips- two explorers and millions of stars, succumbing to the philter of a love so strong it whitens the light in your retinas so that earth appears as heaven must.

Sex changes a man- this is not to say that a man will be forever altered by sex, but there is always that post-coital moment in which a man, for the only times in his adult life, thinks clearly.  In these moments, the universe aligns and all confusion dissipates and he is able to tell himself with absolute truth, usually based on the sex he just had and who he had it with, his current state of being.  This, he knows, is truth, and it will last until his body seeks sex once again.  Kane Hailand, for the first time since Train of Power, was hopeful- distraught, utterly lost, slightly demented- but hopeful.  Kane smiled without knowing why.  Then he laughed because he was smiling.  The beautiful lady lying at his side kissed his cheek.  He returned to feel her warmth, bathing in the stars, the drum, and the ney.  Just the slip of her sweet tongue was enough to send him dazed into the gardens of forever.

Kane:  What is it that makes you need a child?

Alma:  A child knows only what you tell it.  If you give a child nothing but love, then love is the only thing a child will ever know.

Alma rubbed a hand through Kane's ratty hair.  She smiled, and whispered.

Alma:  Jake.

Track 5: Eureka! A Child


Whither dance the hands of time, turning the world to conduct the symphony of life.  The tides sweep ashore, the mountains dance, the lands collide, and layers of dirt pile slowly to bury the ascendants as the mountains gradually disintegrate, for one day the most challenging of summits will become surmountable as we build upon our earth- and satisfaction, we shall see, is a bland plateau.  The sun pursues its parabolic slope- rise and descend, rise and descend, perpetuating through promised continuity.  Days turn to weeks, weeks to months.  Life happens, and as it happens certain things become opaque while others become clear as the truth.

Heart of the People's travelers never counted time.  They did not measure their lives in units, and in fact did not measure their lives at all.  They lived.  Every night, Kane fell asleep in Alma's arms with the same stubborn hope that the sun will not have risen when he again steps into wakefulness.

Alma walked the morning fields alone, watching the sunrise.  Content, she mused that it had been quite a while since they last traveled.  For ages, her feet had steered her in an aimless wander, and now that she had finally found something called life it may be time to stop moving and enjoy it.  Summer was past, its aestival heat only traceable through the settled remnants of their dusty trail.  The world was sure to become colder both in day and in night.  She prepared for the impending cold, but her preparations distracted the group- the lunar cycle elapsed and still they had not bathed.

To prepare for the cold, Alma wove an additional sweater for each of her men.  It had become tradition that she weave new clothing for every change of season, and though her seasonal weaving was never mandated by Heart of the People's code, she had always reaped a sense of worth from sharing her talents with her family.  Years ago, she learned to weave by observing a spider weave its gossamer into a web.  By now she was a virtuoso.  The weaving was cathartic- the hands worked while the brain journeyed.

Alma gave Kane his first sweater just two days after their inaugural romantic encounter.  She cherished their first act of love because, to her, it was the most beautiful dance in the annals of time- primal and holy, encumbered in harmony.

Strumming her lyre through the fields under sunrise, Alma was never happier.  She had never been in love, yet she wasn't eager to say that this was love because the label didn't matter.  What mattered was that she was sure of how she felt.  Although she and Kane had only known each other for a short time, they had known nothing but each other in this time.  A life in solitude has no humdrum- a life in solitude is naked of bollocks.  Making love to Kane had become a nightly occurrence, one she found as gratifying as music.  But Kane was still hesitant to accept her open love.  He required patience.  She anticipated this.  His past was his ever-present burden, and she would need to work tirelessly to relieve him of his sadness.  He had proven himself an attentive lover, communicating effectively with his body, as she always knew he was capable of- and now Alma has grown full.  Not to jinx her luck or to excite prematurely, but the restoration of hope, and not just hope but faith that there was life developing inside her, was the revitalization she so critically needed.  Her health, though not healed, had visibly changed.  Of course she still succumbed to the aches and the nausea, the bleeding and fatigue, but her breasts had become swollen and tender, her nipples now darkened, and as of late she has developed a deep and unlikely craving for beans.  Most poignant, however, was her convalescing vision- the silver spots were not nearly as blinding.  At times she was granted a magical vista free of occlusion, breathing in the land with her beautiful love.  She had been purblind for what must have been years.  Heart of the People always said that Alma's blindness was in the spirit- she was going blind because she did not wish to see.  To rise in the morning with the blessed gift of sight was a daily reawakening in the stately palace of tellurian nature.

She thought of the first time she saw Kane's face.  It was a mystical thrill.  When she was blinded, she tried compulsively to piece together a complete mental portrait of Kane through a combination of the fragments of his image that had shone through her spotted vision and her physical impressions from feeling his face.  When at last she saw him as he appeared, complete and full in his visage, it was as though a prophet had granted life to a beautifully weathered statue.  He was even more handsome than she imagined- rugged beard and toned muscles, Kane's image was more suggestive of a Spartan warrior than a rehabilitating poet.  His face was the face she was meant to be with, the face of man.  This face, she gushed, was the face she would spend her life with, the physical embodiment of the long-awaited pursuit of her one unattained dream of creating a new people- this face will give life to a new breed of men.  Alma, newly enlightened, laden with the divine responsibility of fostering mankind in her womb, displayed a renewed verve when she joined the others with her lyre.

A wolf howled in the distance.  Alma froze.  A wolf can howl in twelve distinct harmonic tones- each tone is a unique signal to the rest of its pack indicating the presence of food, alarm, or danger.  If Kane Hailand had known this when he was recording with The Scoundrels- the world will never know what Howl to Howl could have been.  Alma had no time for such thoughts, petrified still like a mummy, listening for motion in the fields.  She knew the wolves were looking for Kane, that any animal traveling toward any of them was in pursuit of Kane- but Kane was asleep, and any wolf would be disappointed to pass unacknowledged by the sleeping Kane Hailand.  Alma had never been scared of a wolf, but with a stomach soon to be round and full like the world itself, the slightest of risks posed unthinkable fears.  She turned back to wake Kane.

Kane had grown a considerable beard.  He had begun a workout regimen that he believed would keep him healthy and facilitate his pulling of the wagon.  Hank Adaline used to take yoga.  He wanted to have complete control of his body.  This is what ignited him with the fluidity to play one hundred thousand piano notes per hour, averaging to about twenty-eight notes per second- just one of Hank's many world records.  Leon, on the contrary, never believed in physicality.  He says physicality is empty, that imagination will take you wherever you wish to go.  Kane Hailand the performing Jester of the Scoundrels had lived a lazy lifestyle- the only exercise he ever attempted was to avoid the act of exercising.  The newly bearded, nomadic Kane Hailand would appear unrecognizable to Scoundrels fans- The Jester, no one lamented, had died without a funeral.

Kane awoke, hungry, his head lying comfortably on the guitar.  His ears rang in a sustained tone, and he immediately snatched the guitar to pluck the note that matched the tone inside his head.  Following intuition, he nailed the note on his first attempt.  From there, he composed a brief song in the key of the note in his head.  He didn't know the names of the notes he was playing and he couldn't explain how to play music in any particular key, but the music was natural in his blood, and by this point he could fool any passerby into thinking he was a longstanding student of the instrument.  He had learned to play chords, picking harmonies in his head and translating them through his fingers.  In so many ways, it was like penning a song, but the language was his to invent.

Kane had worked several Scoundrels songs to completion on the guitar.  He could unroll Turkish Bath with ease.  He played Death Unto Mnemosyne with a confident mastery.  Alma was particularly fond of this tune, and this gladdened Kane because it was a spiritual song that required a spiritual reaction.  The first time Anika Handel read the lyrics to Death Unto Mnemosyne, she set them down in callous indifference as she expressed her desire to fuck Kane in a cemetery.

Kane began to piece out The Atomic She, but the first verse proved too difficult.  Many of The Scoundrels' songs were unapproachable because of Hank's intricate stylistics.  Hank never fully understood the concept of simple beauty- Kane always thought that just because Hank was damned with the abilities of a genius and the pinpointed dexterity of a neurosurgeon didn't mean he had to show off.  Kane's workings of The Scoundrels' music were never true to how Hank played the songs on record.  Hank Adaline himself couldn't reproduce what he did on record- not that he would be interested in trying.  As The Scoundrels' performances were heavily improvised, Hank never played the same thing twice- Art, he always said, is in the air.  In the studio, Hank and Anika refused to go beyond a single take.  The first time is the right time, make no mistake, and they became bored with a song after just one rehearsal, retiring the tune until their next performance.  Kane never felt this sense of confidence with his lyrics- he reworked every verse until the first draft of honest thought was unrecognizable.  Hank and Anika's refusal to play any song more than once per day often frustrated producers, but the manic culture that followed The Scoundrels rendered them the only Artists in the world to which men in suits were powerless.

Kane's first sight that day was of radiating beautiful Alma.  To be smothered in her scented flesh would be the sweetest death.  She lay beside him.  To feel him lie beside her, to stroke his hair and taste his lips and earn that sparse moment when his exterior cracks and his song fills the air- this was what she lived for.  There was a renewed verve about Alma.  She fashioned her clothes differently.  Her blouse was tighter, leaner- her belly full with life, her breasts magnificent and lovely, hair perfumed to accent her dark shapely brow and her sapphire eyes.  Kane thought it uncharacteristic that Alma would place so much stock in her clothes and her blister-inducing sandals, but Alma just laughed and said it's style, you're either in or you're out.  Alma also said that Spartan women dressed daringly- Spartan women were supposed to be proud of their physical fitness.  Alma, of late, was most definitely proud, harboring the future in her being.  This was the perfect wakeup, exhibiting remarkable potential for a day in which nothing can falter.

The family began their day by traveling, their first move in weeks.  This should have pleased Kane, who was desperate for a bath, and even more desperate for Heart of the People to bathe, but Kane had accidentally wiped his ass with an ivy leaf the night before.  Sweaty, sticky, eyes dry, dirt caked under his fingernails, hair long and greasy, and skin peeling from the soles of his feet (and for some reason his ears as well), Kane pulled the wagon, the guitar slung round his neck, traveling to the glory of music, both hands occupied and unable to scratch his ass.  He was transfixed with Heart of the People's stump, cut just short of the elbow, the wooden contraption tucking the drum at his waist, swinging just above the ever-holstered blade like a pendulum to conduct the rhythm of time.  The stump swayed with the beat of the talking drum, speaking clearer than its owner would ever speak to Kane.

Kane had recently fallen in love with his guitar.  He knew also that Alma loved him.  Both loves were so fresh, so promising and inviting, that he questioned whether he was capable of balancing the two. Perhaps there was a way in which he could combine his two loves- love both Alma and his music.  Success with music and women had always been his only fears, and now, after years of neglect, his fears will teach him love.  As a discontented player in The Scoundrels, Kane spent so much time seeking approval through his art.  Now he was learning the only subject he never studied as a paramount intellectual- his own fulfillment.  He basked in the music as he walked- gravel crunching beneath his filthy beaten sneakers in the mountains as Heart of the People walked barefoot to lead him.

Heart of the People stopped at a slaughtered wolf carcass.  Thick blood oozed from a solitary hole in the wolf's throat- evidence that the wolf had been recently slain.

Alma:  Who would shoot this poor wolf and leave him here?

Leon:  In our code, Kane, it states that if you don't to eat your kill, you must use its assets- its hide or its bones.

Alma:  Killing for the sake of killing- what a shame.

Leon:  Winter is coming, and the wolf has a beautiful coat.

Alma (struck by the stench):  A putrid coat.

Leon:  We must find the man who did this.

Kane:  A man didn't do this.

Leon (laughing):  Yes, Kane, this wolf shot itself.

Leon and Heart of the People exchanged words.  Heart of the People chuckled and pointed a finger to his forehead, a gesture that Kane could only take as a mockery of his own foolish tattoo.

Leon:  Do you know how wolves attack their prey, Kane?

Kane:  No.

Leon:  They bite the perineum.

Kane:  The perineum?

Leon (smiling through his loops of beard):  The gooch.  Bleeding to death at your gooch is no jolly way to go.

Kane:  He wasn't shot.

Leon:  Who?

Kane:  The wolf.

Leon:  Is that so, detective?

Kane:  That's not a bullet-hole.  It's the mark of a fang.

Heart of the People clutched his leopard-fang necklace, though he couldn't possibly have understood Kane's language.  Leon stooped to lift the wolf.

Leon:  Lend me a hand.

Kane:  What are you doing?

Leon:  This is fresh meat- we must follow the code.

Kane and Leon loaded the wolf onto the wagon.  Curiously, Kane noted that, for the first time, he was being called to load the wagon.  At one time, Kane would have shuddered to think of how much weight he was pulling in the wagon, but the new Kane Hailand enjoyed the workout.  He asked Leon the reason behind this new rule to the code- it didn't seem right that a resource must be used just because it's available.  Leon explained that sustenance must always be held as a chief concern.  Life is sacred and it must not be wasted.

Contemporary concerts offer light shows in the form of multi-colored lasers and digital backdrops.  Light technicians do whatever they can with the properties of light.  The Scoundrels were never limited by the properties of light because Kane Hailand made his own laws.  They say that light moves at 186,000 miles per second- whoever says this has never met Kane Hailand.  Kane could cradle light, slow it- speed it even.  He was struck by lightning as a student, and God charged him with a marvelous ability.  When he was struck by lightning once again in the wild, God didn't charge him with any new powers.  Kane thought about setting the sun.  As The Jester, Kane Hailand could probably set the sun.  He never tried.  He was too scared of success.  Now his gift was gone.  He watched his shadow as he pulled the wagon.

Leon dropped back to talk to Kane as Heart of the People navigated barefoot over rocks and twigs, his calloused feet feeling nothing.  Alma, having conquered much of her blindness, walked free without a hand to guide her, independent in the liberty of vision.

Leon:  Humanity is always building its encyclopedia of knowledge.  The more knowledge they acquire, the more they believe that they know about their environment.  The more they know, the greater their illusion of control.  It's similar with your guitar.  The more you practice, the more you believe you know- the more you think you know, the more you feel you're in control.

Kane:  It's a frustrating process.

Leon:  What humanity doesn't realize, however, is that the only reason they have control is because they themselves created the environment they study.  Their control is an illusion they created.  Sure, they didn't create the circumstances of the world, just as you didn't invent the guitar- but they imagine complete control over what happens within the circumstances they have been placed in.  One day you will feel this with your guitar.  That guitar will be your world, your very spirit, your Anima.

Kane:  I don't know, Leon.

Leon:  Out here in nature, away from the society humanity created, we have total control.  We can build as they have built.

Kane:  I thought we didn't want to be like them.

Leon:  What you are doing- fathering a child with Alma- is a very noble deed.  Alma had almost given up before we found you.  Out here, Kane, Alma wishes to establish paradise.  I just want to warn you- don't get too attached to this idea.

Kane:  What's that supposed to mean?

Leon moved ahead.  Something about Leon's demeanor struck Kane as disingenuous.  What Leon said made sense, but there was also a sense in which he couldn't have meant what he said.  Exhausted with thinking, aching and itching, Kane Hailand reverted to thoughts of setting the sun.  Hank Adaline used to be obsessed with mirroring opposites- he used to spend evenings in drunken philosophical discourse, mumbling incoherently, saying that your pupils dilate when you see someone you love as well as when you see someone you hate, and that the light you see in the moon is just a reflection of the sunlight.  Then he'd give you the most compelling argument for believing in a strange relativity between good and evil.  Hank's mirroring of opposites was the initial inspiration behind Train of Power, Kane's 252 line masterpiece told through rhyming couplets by two arch-rival narrators.

Kane followed the promising rush of water in the distance.  Leon stopped him, staring deeply into Kane's face, never altering his expression, inducing a mirror's self-consciousness in Kane.  Kane inhaled sour whiffs of Leon's loopy beard.  After staring blank-faced for a few moments, Leon erupted in laughter.

Leon:  A tattoo on your forehead- you're a crazy fellow, Kane Hailand!

Kane laughed along with Leon, walking under the valley sun.

Kane:  I think you'd like Anika's nickname.

Leon:  Excuse me?

Kane:  Anika's nickname.

Leon:  What is it?

Kane:  The Quaquaversal.  That's what they call Anika Handel.  It means-

Leon:  Moving in all directions from a common center- I know the term.  How does it apply to your Anika?

Kane:  She could do anything with her voice, take you in any direction- make you feel everything at once.

Leon:  Anika, the Quaquaversal.  What was your nickname?

Kane:  I was The Jester.

Leon:  Tell me, clown, why are you still so hung up on past loves when you have Alma in your life?

Kane:  It's not that easy.  Anika consumed me for so long.  Alma's great- she may be the greatest thing that could ever happen to me.  I don't want to mess it up with her like I've messed up everything I've ever been given- but she reminds me so much of Anika.

Leon:  Is that so?

Kane:  The amazing musical abilities, the need for a child- she even looks like Anika used to look before she dyed her hair violet.  I feel like Alma is the perfection Anika would've been if it wasn't for me and Hank, if Anika never became a Scoundrel.

Leon:  If our Alma is a better version of what you left behind, you should quit your mourning.

Kane:  Anika told me things she told no one else.  She said she fantasized about killing Hank.  She wanted to ride his naked body as she watched the life fade from his flashing red eyes- fuck the life out of him.

Leon:  You just betrayed her secret to me, and I never even prompted you to do so.  If you really loved her, you would've kept her secret, even if you were sure that I would never meet her.

Kane:  I told her secret to the world.  I made a song out of it- When You Fade to Red In Me.  I'm the reason Anika has no secrets.

Leon:  When I was in politics, I read a story in which our government sentenced a woman to twelve years in prison for having premarital sex with her fianc.  She was pregnant with her lover's baby, and she gave birth in a penitentiary.

Kane:  That's awful.

Leon:  I read of a woman imprisoned for leaving her father's home to be with her lover, another who was arrested for choosing her own husband without her father's consent.  I even read of a virgin who was put away for the intention of sex.  My point is that you should understand how fortunate you are to have freedom in your courtship.

Kane:  How am I supposed to find comfort knowing that these injustices exist?

Leon:  There's a great word you should know, Kane- chantpleure.

Kane:  Chantpleure?

Leon stroked his beard, walking slowly through the sand so that Kane could maintain pace with the wagon.

Leon:  It means to sing and weep at the same time.  Sometimes it's necessary that we chantpleure.  It's difficult to make sense of our past decisions.  We look back and we can't understand how we could've acted the way that we did.  There are events we place much meaning in at the time of their occurrence, events we consume ourselves with that lose meaning with time.  Then there are events that are seemingly everyday occurrences when they happen, like a walk through the desert with a friend- and we don't realize as it's happening that the moment we're living in will change our lives.

Kane felt so comfortable at that moment that he considered telling Leon what happened to Usman Kanaan.  But Heart of the People stopped the group before a massive dune.  He spoke to Leon.  Leon related to Kane and Alma that Heart of the People claimed to smell pigweeds in full bloom on the other side of the dune.  The one-armed percussionist adored the salt-sour leaf as a dental medicine.  What he did not know, however, was that consumption of the pigweed is ill-advised during pregnancy.

Alma told Kane that sometimes it might be necessary to eat roots.  Sometimes you have to dig beneath the surface to contact survival- the answer isn't always just before your eyes.  And so the group would hike the dune, despite the fact that it was out of their way and it was sunset.  Since the family was forbidden from navigating at night, this excursion would likely delay their bathing another day.

Sucking chunks of ice, they began their ascent.  Heart of the People seemed unfazed, marching upward like a steadfast soldier.  Within moments, the tip of his bald head had sunk beneath the crest of the dune.  Once Alma was certain that Heart of the People was out of sight, she stopped Kane from continuing upwards with the wagon.  With a mischievous wink, she removed the secret vial of perfume from her blouse to hide it securely inside a lambskin sac of stones.  Her skin had become sweaty, slippery, and irritated in the heat, and the glass vial was a nagging presence in her blouse, rubbing irritably under her sensitively swollen breasts- she waited until Heart of the People disappeared from sight because he could never learn that she kept a glass vial, as the possession of glass explicitly violated his code.

Her vial safely hidden, Alma rushed ahead of Leon and Kane.  Kane followed Leon on an upward hike, scattering conversation topics between navigational skills, Leon's theory that the greatest and most misunderstood piece of literature ever written is the United States Constitution, and another one of his theories that an idea is the most dangerous weapon in the world because it is immaterial, untamable, and unquantifiable.  Kane cared for none of it- beads of sweat licking his temples as his breath drew short, pulling the wagon under the sun and through the sand, heavier with every step, breathing the pendulous rhythm of breath, ass itching like mad, praying that Leon would be quiet for just one moment so Kane could gather his concentration.

Through experience, Kane discovered that the wagon moved uphill more efficiently when he pulled in tremendous spurts of energy.  After every fourth beat in his head, he pulled the wagon several more inches through the sand.  One two three four- pull.  One two three four- pull.  A chorus of panting, heaving, struggling to suck thin gasps of air, the sinews tensing in his neck, capillaries thickening and pulsing in his eyes- one two three four- pull.  All thoughts transformed to Alma.  How could he be so selfish, pitying himself for pulling a wagon when this marvelous woman was trudging through the sands with a beautiful child in tow?  How could he be so numb to such an amazing experience?  Everything must be for the child- everything must be for Alma who carried the child.  One two three four- the wagon became stuck in a rut in the sand.  Suddenly the wagon became heavier.  Kane dug his feet into the sand, pulling with every grain of strength and adrenaline and hope in his being, no longer worried about pulling the wagon upward but more focused on maintaining stasis, desperate for the wagon not to roll down and unravel his progress.  He battled the force of the wagon with the near-entirety of his conviction, his feet slipping in the sand as his heart convulsed and his breathing quickened and the blood-flow to his brain lessened to flirt with the point of paroxysm.

Leon froze mid-theory when he noticed what was happening with Kane and the wagon.  Having conditioned his brain in fight or flight response in preparation for the staging of his suicide, Leon sprung to Kane's assistance.

Alas, he was too late.

Desperately, Kane dove after the falling wagon, landing face-first in the gritty sand.  Lifting his sandy face, Kane watched the family's only wagon, containing all of their belongings, all that they had trusted Kane to carry, crash and spill in the heated desert sand.  The pails of ice, the food, the clothing, provisions, the tent, the knives, the stones, the reinforcement parts for their instruments, the barrel of wine, and the wolf's carcass all ensconced in sand- the box body of their only wagon cracked in two from the stress of the crash.

Heart of the People emerged from beyond the crest of the dune to examine the commotion, his eyes flashing their infinite red as he beheld his lowly terror.  Without hesitation, he shuffled hopelessly down the dune, left leg always lower, hollering in frenzied Arabic.  Kane rose, sifting frantic hands through the sand, dashing to help without even dusting his face.  Leon halted Kane.

Leon:  You're not permitted to handle the contents of the wagon.

Leon sidestepped cautiously down the dune.  Kane watched as Leon and his leader cleared the rubble of his mess, wallowing in guilt because this was his fault and he was left with no opportunity for redemption, forced to stand idle and impotent as he watched them attempt to reverse the disorder he created.  A hand on the crown of his head somehow lightened Kane's despair.  He peered into the sun behind him.

Alma:  Mistakes happen.  If we never made mistakes, we'd never learn anything.  We'll be okay.

Heart of the People roared with the thunder of the beast from below.  His eyes flashed and the sky turned red with fury.  In his one hand, he held Alma's vial of perfume high in the red sky.

Heart of the People was challenged with the unnamable- a member of his family had carried a smoothly blown piece of glass, a material not found in nature, a relic from the society they had long ago renounced.  A member of his family who he provided for and who he sacrificed for and whose sins he carried across the land, had betrayed his simple code.  Kane watched those red flashing eyes.  The glass vial dropped and shattered, perfuming the sand where the wagon had tipped, ensuring that the defiled patch of land will forever be stained with Kane's wagon-tipping folly.  Heart of the People brought his one hand to the leopard's fang at his neck.  Kane could think only of Alma and the purity of their child.  He sprinted down the dune with unmatched fervor, crashing at the knees of his leader, praying forgiveness in his useless language.

Kane:  It's mine.  It was me.  I broke the code.  It's my jar!

Eyes closed in a genuflection, Kane raised his palms skyward to his leader, signifying an offer of whatever he possessed- surely the one-armed man will strike with wrathful vengeance, but in that moment Kane would have endured any torture for Alma's protection.  Heart of the People's eyes flashed and Kane felt the flames burn at his torched fingertips, producing ten controlled fires flickering from his fingers as they burned the blood-red candent coals of his hands, a genius pain in his fingerpads, scarring to ensure that he'll relive this burn every time he touches his guitar or his woman.  The pain built and built and Kane was certain his fingers would rot off, but he wasn't sure whether he could stay awake to see his digits crumble into ash before passing out in agony.  This wasn't a dream, nobody will wake.  It was either a loss of total control or a gain of complete mastery.

Alas, the fire didn't spread.  It stayed controlled at his fingertips, flaring in the sky as Kane bowed before Heart of the People.  Kane wished the fire would spread and end it all, for in his haste he couldn't handle the ride, couldn't out-think the bastardly pain.  But Kane needed the pain- needed it more than he needed life.  Kane saw God in his delirious pain.  He tried to speak to God, to say I was once you, I was once God.  His mind flashed back to Train of Power, when the secret shelter of the Violet 9 mysteriously exploded, killing his faith in his god and his self.

Heart of the People extinguished the flames from Kane's fingers.  Kane Hailand passed out in the sand.

Heart of the People and Alma sought help while Leon kept watch over the spilled wagon and Kane's unconscious body.  Alma knew precisely where Heart of the People was leading her- a shepherd, whose name translated to Mountain Song, had been friends with the family since before Alma found her Anima in the lyre.  Mountain Song lived in a clay hut just a mile down the Yunus Trail.  Heart of the People shared a strange intimacy with the shepherd in that they hardly spoke to one another, but when words were exchanged they were noticeably tense, as if the whole interim of silence had been spent deciding which words were necessary.

Mountain Song collected prayers.  There is a religious tradition in which the sons of Jewish fathers travel to Jerusalem to place their folded written prayers in the crevices between the bricks of the western wall of the Old Temple.  It is said among the ancient tribes that this wall has divine significance.  The crevices are finite in space, and the limited space prevents the wall from housing the prayers of all mankind for all its fated years.  To mitigate the issue, a team of rabbis goes to the wall twice a year to collect the folded prayers and bury them at the Mount of Olives.  So, twice a year, Mountain Song treks to the Mount of Olives to dig up the prayers of the people.

Mountain Song and his father were always entertaining children from nearby families, and Alma's greatest delight was to watch the children run through the sand.  Though Mountain Song couldn't play any instruments, he told wonderful stories, and he was such a silly man.  Like Alma, Mountain Song knew how it felt to really be dying- he suffered renal failure after years spent trying to break the world record for the longest continuous stream of urine.  He laughed about it.  He laughed because, though he felt pain, he was dying properly.  Alma had resolved to do the same until Kane ignited her with life.

As she walked the Yunus Trail, however, Alma was petrified of her omnipotent chieftain.  With their food spoiled in the sand, there would be nothing to offer Mountain Song in exchange for his assistance- Heart of the People was a man who considered the acceptance of alms to be injurious to his fundamental code of life, and he was about to ask another man for unreturned assistance.  She wondered what Heart of the People would do to Kane.  Any of their past initiates would have been burned, honeyed, and left for the buzzards.  Surely Heart of the People recognized something unique in Kane Hailand.  Kane saved her.  Her lover took the fall when the blame was hers to claim, even though Heart of the People would never have burned her as he burned Kane.  She wanted nothing more than to comfort Kane, for there was a sense in which to be held in Heart of the People's ill-esteem was somehow more fearful than a duel with the devil- at least with the devil, a man can brace for his inevitable loss.  Alma caressed the budding life inside her belly.  She felt the sudden urge to urinate, an urge which struck more often since the conception.  Heart of the People scowled at Alma.  He knew the vial belonged to her.  Of course he knew.

Alma and Heart of the People reached Mountain Song's abode.  Ever the gracious host, the shepherd saddled four of his strongest camels and trekked to the tipped wagon before sundown.  There they found Kane and Leon lying in the sand, sucking chips of ice, playing music.  Kane's scabbed fingers burned with every note they pressed into the fretboard.  The guitar song was lovely pain.  Pain produced the music.

The troupe rode back to Mountain Song's abode, teasing Kane for never having ridden a camel before.  Kane established an immediate connection with his camel, brushing his hand over the animal's thick matted fur, observing its large teeth through shifting lips, its eyes like black melons.  Saddled upon the camel's hump, Kane was impressed with the steady and peaceful rockabye all the way to Mountain Song's abode.

Following a strange meal of bananas and wolf meat, Mountain Song and his elderly father implored the band to play their music.  Alma poured alcohol over Kane's fingers to disinfect the wounds, striking Kane with such sharp pain he nearly lost his consciousness for the umpteenth time since he abandoned America.  Heart of the People steadfastly refused the wolf's meat, as wolf meat is abnormally tough and therefore harmful to his musical teeth.  He did, however, remove the hide from the vicious creature, cleaning blood from the fur and slitting the skin at the throat to wear as a headdress.  The wolf's lifeless head rested upon Heart of the People's bald pate, effectively placing the one-armed man inside what was once the wolf's neck, giving him the appearance of a one-man totem pole.  Kane thought the wolfskin smelled almost as foul as the cormorant.  He watched Heart of the People devour bananas and kiwi, chomping with his sharp teeth, smelling like dust.  This was the first time Kane had ever witnessed anyone eat a kiwi with bare hands, although by this point Kane had become accustomed to the postprandial sticky fingers.

Mountain Song's wizened father again implored the band to play their music, but Alma left the group to watch the neighborhood children giggle as they chased each other in a circle.  The children looked healthy, and this made her smile.  She was eager for the child inside her to be born with the same blessed health.

Heart of the People was reluctant to show off in front of Mountain Song, but the scale of balance had unfortunately tipped with the wagon, and the drummer was indebted to the shepherd.  Leon rocked the ney, Heart of the People beat the drum, and Mountain Song meditated as Kane watched Alma play Blind Man's Bluff with the children in the moonlight.  With his burnt hands wrapped snugly in Alma's quilt, all should have been perfect.  But something inside of Kane was inexplicably afraid.

Kane Hailand didn't sleep that night.  He waited for Heart of the People to sleep- then he approached the ever-awake Leon about seeking Mountain Song's assistance in building a new wagon for the family.  Leon thought this was an excellent idea.  Mountain Song kindly offered them a wagon of his own, but Kane wisely denied the request, as it was imperative that he be the one responsible for building their new wagon- and besides, Heart of the People could accept no alms outside the family.  Coincidently, Mountain Song had installed an iron gate to house his herd, and his former wooden fence still stood, serving as nothing more than a nuisance.  The shepherd entreated Kane to take wood from the old fence to build his wagon.

Through the night, Leon counseled Kane on woodwork.  Having Kane as a companion in the darkness soothed Leon.  He viewed their wagon-building as an opportunity to seek solace in another, to escape his nightly free-fall into the grating tunnel of waking nightmares.  The wood from Mountain Song's fence was weak, but it would need to be strong enough to survive the toils of carrying materials through the stress of rough terrain, which required Kane to apply sound woodwork to ensure that the wagon wouldn't falter.
